“She was looking at us when she was at the bar, too.”
</p>

<p>
“She was?” Laura was amazed that Marcie would notice
such a thing. “How do you know?”
</p>

<p>
Marcie laughed again. “I was looking at her,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“You what?”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, not the way you think. I was just sort of looking
around and she was looking at our table. I think she
<a class="pagenum" title="44" id="page-44"></a>
wanted to come up and talk to us but she didn’t dare
with the boys there. She knew we weren’t gay.”
</p>

<p>
“Is that what they call it—gay?”
</p>

<p>
“Yes. You know, it gave me the funniest feeling, her
staring at us like that.”
</p>

<p>
Laura turned over in her bed, very wide awake. She
said to herself, <em>I won’t ask her about it</em>, but she couldn’t
help asking. “What sort of feeling?” she whispered.
</p>

<p>
“Well, it was like ... if I tell you you won’t think I’m
like <em>them</em>, will you?”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, no! Of course not.” Laura felt the blood beating in
her throat.
</p>

<p>
“It was like I wanted to know what she’d do to me. If
we were alone, I mean. I was sort of curious. I wondered
what it would feel like. Not that I’d ever let a girl—I
mean—Laura, did you ever kiss a girl?”
</p>

<p>
“No,” Laura said. In the dark she could lie pretty well.
Her blushing cheeks didn’t show.
</p>

<p>
“I did, once.”
</p>

<p>
Laura put her hands to her throat and tried to still her
breathing. “Did you like it?” she whispered.
</p>

<p>
“Not much. But I didn’t dislike it. I was at that age.
She was a friend of mine in Junior High. Maybe she
turned out queer. I mean homosexual. She probably thinks
<em>I</em> turned out queer,” and she laughed. “She was always
wanting to touch tongues.”
</p>

<p>
Laura shivered. “Did you?”
</p>

<p>
“A couple of times. It gave me the creeps. With a man
it’s so lovely.” Laura heard her turn in her bed to face
her. “Didn’t you ever do that when you were little? We
used to do it a lot, just because it felt so awful. But
Lenore was always wanting to do it with me when we
got older. We were sort of best friends for a while.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was sitting up, shivering, on the edge of her bed.
She thought, <em>Dear God, if there is a God, help me now.
Don’t let me touch her. Please don’t let me.</em>
</p>

<p>
Suddenly Marcie got up and crossed the small aisle between
the beds. She felt Laura and sat beside her. “Stick
out your tongue,” she commanded, giggling.
</p>

<p>
“No!”
</p>

<p>
“Come on. I want to feel twelve years old again. I feel
silly. Stick your tongue out.” She was teasing and Laura
<a class="pagenum" title="45" id="page-45"></a>
could see the flash of gold hair in the moonlight that
struck them from the window by the bed.
</p>

<p>
“Marcie, don’t do this! Don’t! You’re playing with fire.
Please, this is crazy.” But her voice dwindled to a whisper
as Marcie took her face in her hands, and she was powerless
to resist. She let herself be pulled toward Marcie, felt
Marcie’s soft wet tongue searching for her own. Laura
opened her mouth with a slight gasp. Her arms went out
to grasp Marcie’s slender body as a groan escaped
her.
</p>

<p>
Suddenly the phone rang. Laura gave a little scream of
shock. They were both utterly silent and motionless until
it rang again. Then Marcie began to laugh. “Oh, wouldn’t
you know!” she said. “Saved by the bell. Saved from a life
of sin.” The phone rang again. “I’ll get it,” Marcie said.
She sprang up from the bed. Laura sat frozen where she
was, hugging herself, trembling and miserable. “It’s probably
Burr wanting to apologize for being such a skunk,”
Marcie said. She threw herself across her bed and lifted
the receiver. “Hello? ... Laura, it’s for you.” She put her
hand over the mouthpiece and said, “It’s Jack.”
</p>

<p>
“I don’t want to talk to him.”
</p>

<p>
“Don’t be silly. Talk to him.”
</p>

<p>
Unwillingly Laura took the phone, sitting on the bed
beside Marcie. She was so conscious of Marcie’s body
stretched out there beside her that she had trouble concentrating
on Jack.
</p>

<p>
He said, “Mother, I’ve been an ass.”
</p>

<p>
“I know.”
</p>

<p>
“Forgive me.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re forgiven,” she said. “Now go to bed. Goodnight.”
</p>

<p>
“But I am in bed,” he said. He was still pronouncing
each word with elaborate care. “My question is this—did
you really mean it?”
</p>

<p>
“Mean what?” said Laura looking at the faint moonlit
curve of Marcie’s leg.
</p>

<p>
“I’d swear you said you loved me,” he said.
</p>

<p>
“You were dreaming.”
</p>

<p>
“Do you?”
</p>

<p>
“No. Jack, please go to bed. Let me go.”
</p>

<p>
“If I went any more to bed than I already am, Mother—and
<a class="pagenum" title="46" id="page-46"></a>
don’t think that was easy to say, because it wasn’t—I
don’t know where I’d be. Say you love me.”
</p>

<p>
“No. Jack, it’s late. I’m tired.”
</p>

<p>
“Tomorrow is Saturday. You can sleep.”
</p>

<p>
“I don’t care what tomorrow is, I’m tired right now.
Now goodnight.”
</p>

<p>
“Do something for me, Mother.”
</p>

<p>
Marcie turned over, lying across her pillow on her stomach.
</p>

<p>
“What?” Laura said softly, losing contact with him.
</p>

<p>
“Promise.”
</p>

<p>
“Okay.” She whispered it.
</p>

<p>
“Kiss Marcie for me.”
</p>

<p>
“<em>What?</em>” Laura was shocked into total awareness.
</p>

<p>
“Goodnight, Mother,” Jack said. And hung up.
</p>

<p>
Laura replaced the receiver and sat uncertainly on the
bed next to Marcie for a minute. She didn’t dare to wonder
what Jack meant. She had enough to do just keeping
her hands off Marcie’s smooth behind. She felt afraid
of her.
</p>

<p>
<em>What would Beth have done if it had been me lying
there?</em> she wondered, and knew at once. Beth would have
laid down on top of her, her front to Marcie’s back. Beth
would have kissed her neck, her ears, her shoulders. Beth
would have—
</p>

<p>
“Laura,” Marcie murmured.
</p>

<p>
“Yes?” Her throat was dry, making it hard to answer.
</p>

<p>
“We’d better get to sleep.”
</p>

<p>
It was all over, then. Laura had waited too long. Maybe
Marcie would have repulsed her anyway. Maybe her hesitation
had saved her. On the other hand, maybe—Laura
burned to know. But Marcie had lost the playful, childish,
experimental mood, and was already half asleep. There
might never be another chance.
</p>

<div class="chapter">

<h2 class="chapter" id="chapter-0-6">
<a class="pagenum" title="47" id="page-47"></a>
Chapter Six
</h2>

</div>

<p class="first">
<span class="firstchar">A</span><span class="postfirstchar">t</span> work on Monday Laura’s phone rang halfway
through the morning. “Doctors Hollingsworth, Carstens,
and Hagstrom,” she said, business-like. What a mouthful!
she thought to herself.
</p>

<p>
Her listener apparently had the same idea. “Jesus, what
a tongue-twister,” he said. “What happened to Smith?”
</p>

<p>
“There is no Dr. Smith,” she said, taken aback.
</p>

<p>
“Oh, don’t be so damn formal, Mother. It’s not like
you. I thought I’d better apologize while I’m sober. I was
drunk the last time.”
</p>

<p>
“I know. How are you, Jack?” She smiled at the thought
of his face.
</p>

<p>
“Bored. But healthy. I didn’t mean to fall asleep in your
face Friday night.”
</p>

<p>
“It’s okay. Forget it.”
</p>

<p>
“Just for that I’ll give you a free ticket to see my
analyst. He’s a great guy. He needs you.”
</p>

<p>
“He needs me?”
</p>

<p>
“Have you got fifty bucks a week to spend on your salvation?”
</p>

<p>
“I haven’t got fifty bucks a week to spend on my
<em>groceries</em>,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“Well, I guess he doesn’t need you as much as I thought.
But I’d be glad to stake you to your first session. After
that it becomes habitual. You crave it. You’ll find the
money somehow.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was laughing. “Give it to Marcie, not me,” she
said. “She’s the one who loves to talk.”
</p>

<p>
“You do some pretty good talking yourself.”
</p>

<p>
“I do?”
</p>

<p>
“You got lyrical in defense of oddballs Friday night.”
</p>

<p>
“I did not! Let’s not go into that again anyway,” she
said. “Look, Jack, I’d like to talk to you, but—”
</p>

<p>
“I know, you’re at work. So am I. Don’t you ever get
tired of work?”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="48" id="page-48"></a>
“I’m on probation here. If I don’t do well they’ll fire
me in June.”
</p>

<p>
“So your poor virtuous hardworking little life revolves
around that office.”
</p>

<p>
“Now you’re being an ass again.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m telling you, Laura, you’d make a good soap opera.
So would the rest of us. We’re all a bunch of nuts in a
million nutsy little soap operas. Will Burr marry Marcie?
Will Jack take the pledge? Will Laura stick it out till
June? Tune in tomorrow. We won’t have the answer for
you, but we’ll sell you soap like all hell. Do you know
why people buy soap?”
</p>

<p>
“To wash themselves.”
</p>

<p>
“No. They like to play with themselves in the bathtub.”
</p>

<p>
Laura had to laugh at him. “You fool,” she said. “Jack,
I can’t talk to you, honestly.”
</p>

<p>
“Okay. I’ll call back.”
</p>

<p>
“No, no<a id="corr-2"></a>, call tonight.”
</p>

<p>
“But I want to see you tonight.”
</p>

<p>
She was unaware that she might have impressed him
on their date, and he took her by surprise. “You do?”
</p>

<p>
“Well, don’t sound so damn shocked. You’re a nice girl
even if you are ten feet tall. I’ll pick you up at seven-thirty.”
</p>

<p>
“No, I can’t, Jack.”
</p>

<p>
“Okay, eight.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m busy.”
</p>

<p>
“The hell you are.”
</p>

<p>
“I am.”
</p>

<p>
“You lie! I have an instinct about these things. Eight
sharp.”
</p>

<p>
All at once Laura became aware of another voice calling
her. “Laura?” It was Dr. Hollingsworth. He was standing
over her desk and she looked up suddenly like a scared
little kid.
</p>

<p>
“Yes, sir?” she said. She hung up without even saying
goodby to Jack.
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
Jack Mann was not a pushy type. On the contrary, he was
rather shy, although it rarely showed. He went to parties
and hid behind a stream of wisecracks. He did the same
thing on dates. He did it with anyone and everyone. It
<a class="pagenum" title="49" id="page-49"></a>
was a sort of defense mechanism, a way of hiding his real
self, and he had done it for so many years that by now
it was second nature. Even people who knew him fairly
well, like Burr and Marcie, never saw beneath this facade
of witticism. They thought that <em>was</em> Jack: all funny asides
and not much serious straight talk. It was hard to take
him seriously. He didn’t want that. He wanted to be
laughed at, to be amusing, and he usually contrived to be.
He was content to let people take him for a wag.
</p>

<p>
But once in a while he ran across somebody who made
him feel sick of the mask he hid behind. Somebody who
made him yearn to talk, quietly and seriously, about the
things that mattered to him. It happened when he was unlucky
enough to fall in love. Or when he met a loner like
himself and felt an unspoken sympathy. It happened with
Laura.
</p>

<p>
It wasn’t easy for him to call her back. It would have
been, if she hadn’t appealed to his emotions. There were a
lot of girls he called just for the sake of their mutual
amusement, or just to amuse himself. But if he bantered
lightly with Laura it was more because he couldn’t help it
than because he wanted to, more because he had found it
almost impossible to talk straight anymore.
</p>

<p>
At eight o’clock he showed up at the apartment. He
walked into the living room without knocking and said,
“It’s me.” When nobody answered he wandered through
to the bedroom and found Laura giving Marcie a home
permanent. “God, what a stink!” he said.
</p>

<p>
Laura looked up in surprise. “I thought you were kidding,”
she said. “About the date.”
</p>

<p>
He smiled at her. She was dressed in tight chinos and a
boy’s shirt. It was her favorite after work outfit. “You
weren’t planning on the Stork Club, I see,” he cracked.
</p>

<p>
“I wasn’t planning on anything.”
</p>

<p>
“That’s a dangerous attitude, Mother. Always plan
on something. Avoid accidents.”
</p>

<p>
“Jack, you can’t have her,” Marcie said. “I’ll never
get this thing right without her.” She waved a plastic
curler at him helplessly.
</p>

<p>
“You’re better off without it, doll. Take it from me.
Come on, we’ll go as you are.”
</p>

<p>
“Jack, I can’t. I had no idea—”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="50" id="page-50"></a>
“Come on. I want to talk to you.”
</p>

<p>
“We can talk here.”
</p>

<p>
“No we can’t. Marcie’s here.”
</p>

<p>
“I won’t listen,” Marcie said with a smile.
</p>

<p>
“Besides, I can’t talk,” he said, and Laura caught a
glimpse of the shyness hidden in him. “I’m sober as
a post.” He shrugged. “Let’s go.”
</p>

<p>
“I can’t go like this.”
</p>

<p>
“Never mind the pants, they’re becoming.”
</p>

<p>
For some deep buried and curious reason she was flattered.
She stood there hesitating and Jack took advantage
of her. He grabbed her arm and pulled her toward
the door.
</p>

<p>
“Kidnapper!” Marcie wailed.
</p>

<p>
“My coat—” Laura said.
</p>

<p>
“You don’t need one. It’s balmy.”
</p>

<p>
“So are you.”
</p>

<p>
“Thanks.” He guided her down the steps.
</p>

<p>
This was a switch for Laura. She had never been especially
attractive to men before, starting at the beginning
with her father and going right on up through
college. She didn’t look warm and soft and yielding.
She was remote and involved in herself, aloof from everybody,
men included. She didn’t like them very much
and they sensed it.
</p>

<p>
Now, here was a well educated intelligent male giving
her the rush. She didn’t understand it. Jack didn’t
appeal to her physically any more than any other man;
in fact, a little less. He was small, wiry, rather owlish
in his horn rims. He looked like an ivy league undergrad.
She guessed he was about twenty-five. Laura was
twenty. But she supposed that in five or six years
she might be as cynical as Jack was. She liked to hear
him say things she never dared to say herself.
</p>

<p>
They went to a little bar a few blocks away where Laura
had gone once with Marcie for a beer. It was a quiet
spot with a steady clientele.
</p>

<p>
They walked in and took a booth in the back. “I usually
prefer the bar,” said Jack, “but I always end up telling
my troubles to the bartender. So we’ll sit back here.”
</p>

<p>
Laura felt a little strange walking into a bar in a
pair of pants, but she was with a man and she hoped
that made it all right.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="51" id="page-51"></a>
“Just a beer,” she said to Jack. A year ago she would
have said. “Just a coke.” And said it in a way to
make him think she disapproved of liquor. But lately
she had picked up a taste for beer. Beth liked it and
so did Marcie. That was too much for Laura. There
must be something to it. So she had gotten into the
habit of having one now and then in the evening when
she got home from work. It relaxed her. It made her feel
that she could think of Merrill Landon without exploding,
or of Marcie without crawling out of her skin.
She felt like maybe she could stand it, living this way
with Marcie, and everything would turn out all right.
</p>

<p>
The waitress brought their drinks, and Jack poured
her beer for her. Then he downed his shot and drank
some water. He seemed to be looking for a way to talk
to her. “How long have you and Marcie been together?”
he said finally.
</p>

<p>
<em>That’s an odd way to put it</em>, Laura thought warily.
“Since January,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“Oh, yeah. I guess Burr mentioned it. He likes you.”
He smiled at her and she relaxed a little.
</p>

<p>
“Why don’t Burr and Marcie get along?” she asked.
</p>

<p>
Jack shrugged and hailed the waitress. Then he looked
at Laura. “They don’t want to,” he said. “It would spoil
the fun.”
</p>

<p>
“They love each other,” said Laura.
</p>

<p>
“Physically, yes, they do.”
</p>

<p>
Laura didn’t like his definition. “Marcie says they might
get married again.”
</p>

<p>
“Yeah. They’re just blind enough to do it, too.”
</p>

<p>
“Marcie’s not blind!”
</p>

<p>
“Sorry. A slip of the tongue.” He grinned and drank
the fresh drink the waitress had just delivered.
</p>

<p>
“Well, she’s not,” Laura said, disconcerted by his
manner. “She really loves Burr—at least, she thinks she
does.”
</p>

<p>
He put his glass down. “Marcie hasn’t learned to
love yet,” he said.
</p>

<p>
“You mean she doesn’t love Burr?” She asked the question
eagerly.
</p>

<p>
“No,” he said quietly, studying her. “She loves physical
excitement. She loves a big virile passionate sonofabitch
to make a fuss over her.”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="52" id="page-52"></a>
“You’re wrong,” she said, disappointed. “She doesn’t
fight just for the sake of fighting. It’s just with Burr.
She never fights with me.”
</p>

<p>
Jack laughed a little, privately. “That’s because
you’re a girl, Mother,” he said. “I can tell ’cause you
got long hair.”
</p>

<p>
Laura began to sweat under his searching eyes. “That’s
not the point,” she said, exasperated. “Marcie has a
sweet disposition. She’s very quiet. It must be Burr.”
</p>

<p>
“Quiet?” Jack laughed. “The way that girl talks she’s
about as quiet as Grand Central during rush hour.”
</p>

<p>
“All right, she talks a lot.” Laura was getting mad.
“That doesn’t mean she likes fights. Or men who thrive
on them.”
</p>

<p>
“That’s just what it does mean. Believe it. It’s true.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re screwy.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re in love.”
</p>

<p>
“What?” She said it in a shocked whisper, staring
at him, feeling her cheeks go scarlet. “What does
that mean?” she said. Her voice was dry and small and
her hands were wet.
</p>

<p>
Jack drank another shot. Then he put the glass down
and leaned toward her over the table, his face serious.
“You’re gay, Laura.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was speechless for a moment, surprised beyond
her capacity to think or feel. Then an awful sick trembling
came up in her throat. For a minute she hung
between flight and a fight. She was furious, scared and
humiliated. It never occurred to her to deny the truth.
Jack had hit the bull’s eye. She clenched her fists on the
table top and violent things came to her lips. But before
she could utter them Jack spoke again.
</p>

<p>
“Oh, don’t look so damn mad. You’re not the only
one.” He sighed, crushing his cigarette in a scorched
ashtray. “I am too. So don’t give me a martyr act.” And
he nodded again to the waitress.
</p>

<p>
Laura put her hands over her face suddenly, pressing
one hand over her mouth to catch the sobs. She heard the
waitress come up and turned her head to the wall.
</p>

<p>
“Same for me,” Jack said.
</p>

<p>
“How about the lady?”
</p>

<p>
“Bring her a double whisky.”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="53" id="page-53"></a>
Laura could feel the woman looking at her curiously.
She wanted to evaporate. She hated the impersonal curiosity
of this stranger. After a minute Jack said, “She’s
gone.”
</p>

<p>
Laura put her hands down, but she couldn’t look at
him. She just said, rather hopelessly, “How did you
know?”
</p>

<p>
“Takes one to know one,” he said with light sarcasm.
</p>

<p>
“<em>How?</em>” Laura demanded. “You’re a man.”
</p>

<p>
“So I’m a man. You’re a girl. We’re both queer.”
</p>

<p>
“How did you know?” she said sharply, looking at
him now.
</p>

<p>
“You’ve got a crush on Marcie. That’s how.”
</p>

<p>
Laura gasped a little. “Is it so obvious?” she asked,
frightened.
</p>

<p>
Jack shook his head. “To me, maybe, but only because
I was looking for it.”
</p>

<p>
“You were? Why?”
</p>

<p>
“I’m always looking for it.” He was bantering
again. She realized now that he had called her Laura
when he said it: <em>You’re gay, Laura</em>. He was dead serious
then.
</p>

<p>
“Do you look for it even in girls?” she said.
</p>

<p>
“In anybody. You might say it’s a hobby with me. I
spot one and I think to myself, ‘Another poor bastard
like me.’ It boosts my morale. I guess it’s a case of misery
loves company.”
</p>

<p>
“I’d rather suffer alone,” she said, not without pride.
</p>

<p>
“You’ll get over that. When you learn your way around.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was still trembling all over. “Listen to me,
Jack,” she said, leaning over the table and brushing
the last tear impatiently from her cheeks. “I never
heard that word—gay—like you use it until our date
Friday. Nobody ever called me ‘gay’ before. I didn’t
even know what it meant. But I’ll tell you this: I never
touched Marcie. I’ve never tried to get away with anything
with her. Never. She doesn’t know and she never
will.” She said this almost fiercely, but Jack only smiled
at her.
</p>

<p>
“Okay,” he said. “Don’t preach at me. I believe you.
I believe you haven’t been climbing into Marcie’s
bed after hours, anyway. But don’t let Marcie fool you.
<a class="pagenum" title="54" id="page-54"></a>
She can be wild sometimes. She gets in crazy moods and
she’ll do anything. I saw her go up to a bum in Central
Park and kiss him once on a dare. A big ugly slob of a guy.
It was enough to get her killed, but she loved it.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was revolted. But not surprised. Not after what
Marcie had done to her last Friday night.
</p>

<p>
“She couldn’t possibly suspect me,” she said stubbornly.
“I never do a thing.”
</p>

<p>
“You did plenty in The Cellar Friday night.”
</p>

<p>
“I—I did?” She felt that old sick feeling come over
her again.
</p>

<p>
“You looked at her like you had ideas. You held
the door for her. You argued with Burr.”
</p>

<p>
“But all I said was—”
</p>

<p>
“All you said was you were gay. To anybody who
bothered to figure it out. Well, I did, that’s all.”
</p>

<p>
Laura’s face was hot and she tried to defend herself.
“All I did was defend them—homosexuals, I mean. I
just said they were human beings, not animals. Is that
against the law?”
</p>

<p>
“Not ‘they,’ Mother,” Jack said softly. “‘We.’ You’re
one of us.”
</p>

<p>
“But Burr and Marcie don’t know that,” she said, almost
pleading with him to agree.
</p>

<p>
Jack raised a finger to his lips. “Everybody will know it
if you don’t keep your voice down,” he said. “Okay, Burr
doesn’t know it. As for Marcie, you live with the girl.
You should know.”
</p>

<p>
“She couldn’t,” Laura said, but she felt shaky.
</p>

<p>
“You aren’t sure, are you? Why don’t you find out?”
</p>

<p>
“How?” She looked at him eagerly.
</p>

<p>
“Ask her.” He laughed to see her face fall.
</p>

<p>
“Damn you!”
</p>

<p>
“Okay. Don’t ask her. Make a pass at her.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re mad!” Laura stared at him, shocked. “I’d
never do such a thing! She might—why, my God, she
might call the police. Or Burr. She’d hate me. I couldn’t
stand it. Jack, don’t tell me to make a fool of myself. Do
you want to get me into a tragic mess?”
</p>

<p>
“I want to keep you <em>out</em> of one, Mother. That’s why
I’m talking this way.”
</p>

<p>
“Well, act like you did, then. You drag me down
<a class="pagenum" title="55" id="page-55"></a>
here, when I didn’t want to come in the first place,
and tell me—” She swallowed convulsively. “—tell me
I’m <em>gay</em>.” She spat the word at him. “And then you have
the gall to sit there and tell me to make love to Marcie—”
She almost choked on the words. “—when you know
damn well she’d probably be revolted by it, she’d—”
</p>

<p>
“Calm down, Mother, have a drink.” And he held
her glass up to her until she took it from him. It
burned her throat but she was too worked up to care.
</p>

<p>
“Now,” said Jack. “I asked you down here to tell you
I know you’re gay. And so am I. I want to be friends
with you, Laura.” She glanced up at him, and found
that he was embarrassed, for all the liquid courage he
was consuming, and it was hard for him to talk.
“Damn it,” he said. “I’m so used to talking like an
idiot, I can’t say what I mean anymore. I—I wanted
to tell you—to warn you, Laura—I was in the situation
you’re in now. Once. Long ago. I fell for a roommate
of mine, I didn’t think he knew anything either. I
didn’t see how it was possible. I was so damn careful.
I never said anything, I never <em>did</em> anything. Jesus
Christ, I even avoided the guy. I went out of my way to
avoid him. But I was nuts about him, Laura. I wanted
him so bad it hurt. I’d lie there in the dark and tell
myself, ‘You can’t have him, you can’t have him’ over
and over. I’d say, ‘Who the hell do you think you are?
If you were the prettiest boy on earth he wouldn’t look
twice. And, Mann, you aren’t the prettiest boy on earth.
You’re the ugliest.’ Whenever we talked late at night,
whenever we went anywhere together, whenever we
touched I used to burn up inside, I used to die of it. I
wanted to kiss him, feel him all over, just hold him.
God, it killed me! But I never let on. Never. And one
day—this’ll give you an idea of what to expect,
Mother—one day after all this noble chastity and virtue
and self-denial crap I was going through, he came
up to me and said, ‘Jack, I hate to say this, but I’m
moving out.’”
</p>

<p>
“Why?” Laura asked softly, her forehead wrinkled
with sympathy.
</p>

<p>
“Because I was gay. He didn’t say it that way, of
course. He didn’t say, ‘Because you’re queer, you poor
<a class="pagenum" title="56" id="page-56"></a>
bastard. I’m sorry for you but I can’t take it any more.’ I
could have stood that. But he just handed me a lot of
bull.”
</p>

<p>
Laura felt like crying again. But she only said,
“Isn’t that something to be grateful for? He tried, anyway.”
</p>

<p>
“Yeah. He tried.” He said it so acidly that Laura
was afraid to say any more.
</p>

<p>
“There’ve been a lot since Joe,” he said, after a
while. “Just like there’ll be a lot for you after Marcie.”
Laura tried to protest but he waved her down. “I know,
I know, you’re going to keep hands off. You’re going to
spend your whole life ignoring sex, ignoring what you are.
Denying that you want it, running away from it. I
was going to, too. That was twenty-five years ago.”
</p>

<p>
“Twenty-five years ago!” Laura stared at him. “How
old are you, Jack?”
</p>

<p>
“Forty-two. Surprised?” He smiled at her gaping
astonishment.
</p>

<p>
“I thought you were maybe five years older than I
am. Twenty-five or so. You <em>can’t</em> be forty-two.”
</p>

<p>
“That’s what I keep telling myself. I can’t be. But I
sure as hell am.”
</p>

<p>
“I don’t believe it.”
</p>

<p>
He grinned. “Good,” he said. “I like to fool people.”
</p>

<p>
“Why?” It seemed crazy. “What does a man care how
young he looks? I thought that was for women,” Laura
said.
</p>

<p>
“Women and gay boys. Do you think some pretty
twenty-year-old is going to fall for a fat bald, middle-aged
bastard with not even a bankroll to offer? I’m
ugly, Mother. That’s enough of a handicap. When I
start looking old, I’ll quit.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re not ugly, Jack,” she said gently, trying to
console him.
</p>

<p>
But he took it with a sardonic laugh. “Only a
Mother could love me,” he said.
</p>

<p>
“Don’t talk like this. You make me so sad.”
</p>

<p>
“Ahhh, Christ,” he said, and drank. He looked up at
Laura, and she could see he wasn’t focusing very well
now. “I came here to talk about you anyway, not me.
What did your father do to you when you were five
years old?”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="57" id="page-57"></a>
Laura started. “When did I say that? Did I mention
my father?” she asked.
</p>

<p>
“Yes. In The Cellar. Probably as good a place as any.”
</p>

<p>
“I—I didn’t mean <em>my</em> father,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“Don’t fib to me, Laura. Let’s be friends.”
</p>

<p>
After a few moments, she said, “I can’t talk about it,
Jack.”
</p>

<p>
“What did he do?”
</p>

<p>
“We—we were at a summer resort.” It began to spill
out of her. Jack had bared his anguish, and she felt
suddenly safe with him, and needed. “We were there
for a vacation one summer. We went fishing on the
lake—Father and Mother and my brother and me.” Her
voice grew soft as she spoke. “The boat capsized. I was
the only one he could save. I was the closest to him.
Mother and Rod drowned.” She shut her eyes with a little
gasp against the old horror, still so sharp in her
heart, like a big ugly needle stuck there to remind her
she had no right to be alive. “All my life I’ve felt as
if I killed them. He says I did. He hates me because
I’m not his son. He hates me because I’m not my own
mother, his wife.” Jack seemed completely sober for a
minute, staring at her with his brows knit. She put
her head down and cried quietly. “That’s all,” she said.
“I can’t tell you any more.”
</p>

<p>
“You don’t need to,” he said softly. “Jesus.”
</p>

<p>
She took a deep breath and sat up, feeling as if she
had lightened the weight of that leaden secret by sharing
it. She was somewhat surprised to find that she
was able to share it. She felt very close to Jack, as if
they were now truly friends. Each of them had risked a
little of himself to the other. And neither, now, was
sorry.
</p>

<p>
With a sigh, she looked at her watch. It was getting
late. “I have to get up early,” she said, her voice still
unsteady. “So do you. I know, Burr’s always yelling about
the hours. Let’s go.”
</p>

<p>
“One more,” he said, holding up his glass. “I’m
not quite through with you yet.”
</p>

<p>
“I don’t get it,” Laura said to him. “We don’t even
know each other. This is the second time we’ve seen
each other. And here we are talking like old friends.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re wrong, Laura. We know each other a lot better
<a class="pagenum" title="58" id="page-58"></a>
than some people who’ve been acquainted for years.
Like Burr and Marcie. We know each other instinctively,
don’t you feel that? I wouldn’t have called you
otherwise. You wouldn’t have let me drag you out tonight
otherwise.”
</p>

<p>
“Don’t talk about Marcie as if she didn’t have a
brain in her head,” Laura said.
</p>

<p>
Jack smiled. “You <em>are</em> in love,” he said. “This is
serious. She has a few brains, Mother, she just doesn’t
use them.”
</p>

<p>
“She’s not stupid,” Laura defended her eagerly.
</p>

<p>
“She’s not sacred either.”
</p>

<p>
“I didn’t mean that.”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, yes you did. I thought Burr was once.”
</p>

<p>
“Burr?” Laura stared at him. “Did you—were you—”
</p>

<p>
“Nuts for Burr? Yes. Once. When I first met him.”
</p>

<p>
“What happened?”
</p>

<p>
“Nothing. Thank God. I got over it. I go for big
virile sons of bitches, just like Marcie. But I take care not
to room with them anymore.”
</p>

<p>
Laura shook her head, a wry little smile on her face.
“Don’t you ever fall for the gay ones?”
</p>

<p>
“I try to make a point of it.” He grinned sadly. “Unfortunately
I sometimes miss the point. If you know what
I mean.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m not sure.”
</p>

<p>
“It’s just as well. I met Burr at work when he was hired
about four years ago. I knew he was straight.”
</p>

<p>
“What’s ‘straight’?”
</p>

<p>
“Everything that’s not ‘gay’—so I pussyfooted around
the issue. I made him like me. I did his homework for
him. I made him laugh. I told him what to tell the boss.
I double-dated with him and Marcie and I was an usher
at the wedding. I was Number One trouble-shooter <em>after</em>
the wedding. He thinks I’m indispensable.”
</p>

<p>
“But you—you don’t still—”
</p>

<p>
“No, I don’t. Not anymore. But I still like to be with
him. I like to watch them fight.” He smiled at her. “You
do too.”
</p>

<p>
She felt embarrassed, as if he were looking through her
clothes to her naked feelings.
</p>

<p>
“However,” he went on, “I’m not under any lovely illusions
<a class="pagenum" title="59" id="page-59"></a>
about him being an intellectual giant. Or Marcie
either. And I know damn well he won’t give me a tumble
if I just stick around long enough.” He gave her a
piercing look. “Those are your illusions, Mother,” he said.
“I suggest you drown them.”
</p>

<p>
“What does that mean?”
</p>

<p>
“You can set Marcie’s hair till the moon turns blue and
she’s not going to crawl into your bed to thank you for
it.”
</p>

<p>
“I don’t expect her to.”
</p>

<p>
“Sure you do. It’s a mark of our breed. We’re hopeless
optimists. Otherwise we’d all commit suicide. We get
a crush on somebody, and if he’s straight we figure we’ll
just love him so much he’ll <em>have</em> to turn gay. It doesn’t
work that way. Marcie isn’t going to start kissing you
just because you want her to.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was incensed. “She already has,” she snapped.
</p>

<p>
Jack’s eyebrows went up. “When?” he said.
</p>

<p>
“The night we got back from The Cellar. She said she
felt funny in there with all those girls staring at her. She
said she used to touch tongues with a girl when she was
in her teens, and she wanted to do it again. And she did.
You—you—” She didn’t know what to call him. “You
had to call me up right in the middle, just when I
thought—” She stopped herself. “Oh, this isn’t like me,”
she moaned. “I never talk like this.”
</p>

<p>
“Only to yourself, hm?” He laughed. “I mess up your
daydreams and your affair. God! What more can I do?
I’m becoming the Man in your Life, Mother.” His laughter
fizzled slowly, and Laura could tell he was quite drunk
from the way he let his head hang for a minute. “You
know,” he said and wagged a finger at her, articulating
cautiously, “I never have trouble thinking when I’m drunk.
But my tongue gets sloppy.” He laughed a little. “I say
what I want to say, that’s one good thing. But it sounds
sloppy as hell.” He finished the drink in front of him.
Laura started to get up, but he caught her wrist and
said, suddenly very serious again, “You’re in trouble,
Laura. Marcie’s straight. Accept that. It’s a fact. If she’s
playing games with you, she’s doing it for private kicks,
not to give you a thrill. And her kicks have nothing to do
with being gay. They have to do with going out on a
<a class="pagenum" title="60" id="page-60"></a>
limb, with acting nuts once in a while. Maybe she’s just
pushing <em>you</em> out on a limb to see if you’ll fall off. And
you’ll fall all right. Flat on your can.” He stopped for a
minute to focus his gaze on her. “Marcie’s about as
queer as Post Toasties, Laura. Take my advice: move
out.”
</p>

<p>
“But I can’t! I won’t!” she exclaimed defensively. “Why
should I? I’ve done nothing wrong!”
</p>

<p>
“I hadn’t either when Joe gave me the glad news.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m not you. I’ve done nothing I’m ashamed of.”
</p>

<p>
“No, but you will. If you hang around, feeling like you
feel. You were saved by a phone call Friday night. What
if my timing isn’t so good next time?”
</p>

<p>
“I don’t want to move out.” She said it stubbornly like
a thwarted child.
</p>

<p>
“All the more reason why you should.”
</p>

<p>
Laura got indignant. “I’ve got more will power than
you give me credit for and I’m not going to be scared
out.”
</p>

<p>
“What are you going to do for will power if she gets
cold some night and crawls into your bed to keep warm?
Or you take a shower together? Or she feels like pulling
your nerves out by the roots one by one again, and makes
you play let’s-touch-tongues just for the hell of it? Just to
see if she can get you sent up for sodomy? Be thankful
you’re female, Mother. At least your passion won’t stand
up and salute her.”
</p>

<p>
“I’ve had enough from you tonight!”
</p>

<p>
“Okay, okay. But I advise you to find a nice butch
somewhere and set up housekeeping in the Village. Or
at least, cultivate a few lovelies down there so you’ll have
a place to let off steam when Marcie feels like playing
games.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re drunk and repulsive.”
</p>

<p>
“I know what I am, Laura. Don’t change the subject.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m going home.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m coming with you.” He got up unsteadily and followed
her toward the door. Outside he stopped her.
“Don’t hate me, Laura,” he said. And she couldn’t, looking
at him there in the pink glare of neon, short and
plain, brilliant and miserable, offering her his curious
stinging sympathy.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="61" id="page-61"></a>
“I wish I could,” she said and shook her head.
</p>

<p>
He smiled at her. “I’ll walk you home,” he said.
</p>

<p>
“You don’t need to. Why don’t you get a taxi and get
yourself home?”
</p>

<p>
“Are you suggesting I can’t walk?”
</p>

<p>
“No.” She laughed.
</p>

<p>
“You are. Just for that I’m going to walk you home
whether you like it or not. To prove I can.”
</p>

<p>
“All right.” But she had to lead him most of the way.
Jack could talk better than he could navigate when he was
high. When they reached the apartment she hailed a cab
for him and put him in it. She stood there watching it
pull away down West End Avenue, watching it till it was
indistinguishable from the sea of red tail lights traveling
with it. And she felt an awakening affection for him.
</p>

<div class="chapter">

<h2 class="chapter" id="chapter-0-7">
<a class="pagenum" title="62" id="page-62"></a>
Chapter Seven
</h2>

</div>

<p class="first">
<span class="firstchar">I</span><span class="postfirstchar">t</span> was hard to get up the next day. Not impossible, just
hard. Discouraging. Her head ached, and she was dissatisfied
with herself. For the first time she wanted to
cut work. But she went and she did her job. It wasn’t
until the middle of the afternoon that she jumped to hear
Sarah ask, “What’s the matter, Laura? A little under the
weather?”
</p>

<p>
Laura looked up at her. <em>Do I look that bad?</em> she wondered.
“I’m a little tired. Why?”
</p>

<p>
“The reports are piling up,” Sarah said, nodding at
them.
</p>

<p>
Laura rubbed her forehead. “I’m sorry, Sarah. I’ll
catch up. I’ll work late.”
</p>

<p>
“Don’t be silly!” Sarah laughed good-naturedly. “Catch
up tomorrow. There’s not that much of a rush.”
</p>

<p>
But the next day she didn’t quite catch up; she got
farther behind, in fact. Burr and Marcie had kept her
up. It was partly the quarrel and partly the torturing silence
that followed it. She went to work still more tired
than the day before. Dr. Carstens came in to tell her a
story about one of his woman patients, and she was
frankly irritated. He picked himself up from her desk,
where he was sitting, and huffed out, offended. “Okay,
don’t laugh,” he said. “The others thought it was funny.”
</p>

<p>
Laura drove herself almost crazy with her errors that
afternoon. When her phone rang she jumped half out of
her chair. It was Jack.
</p>

<p>
“Good afternoon, Mother,” he said. “I’m selling used
tooth brushes. Interested?”
</p>

<p>
“No. I’m very busy. Goodby.”
</p>

<p>
“I’ll see you at eight.”
</p>

<p>
“No.”
</p>

<p>
“Eight-fifteen.”
</p>

<p>
“No.”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="63" id="page-63"></a>
“Eight-thirty.”
</p>

<p>
“All right! All right! All right! Goodby!” She slammed
the receiver down and Sarah stared at her.
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
Laura decided to work late, and it was close to eight-thirty
when she got up to go. The reports, though fewer,
were still not done.
</p>

<p>
At the elevator the boy said, “Nice evening.”
</p>

<p>
“Is it?” She answered him apathetically, involved in
her own world.
</p>

<p>
“Yes, ma’am. It’s really spring tonight.” He smiled at
her.
</p>

<p>
He was right. The air was soft and gentle, lavender and
clear. It even smelled good, right there in mid-Manhattan,
although that was probably an hallucination.
Laura smiled a little. She hated to go underground to the
subway, but it was late, and she wanted to get home in a
hurry. It would really be gorgeous out on the roof tonight.
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
She walked in to find Jack and Burr playing checkers.
Marcie was cross-legged on the floor, in velvet lounging
pants and a silk shirt, humming while she covered the top
of the round cocktail table with a plastic veneer
treated to look like marble.
</p>

<p>
She smiled up at Laura, who paused to admire her.
“Alcohol proof,” Marcie said, rattling the table cover.
“Mr. Marquardt gave it to me. We’re advertising it for a
new client, and they passed some around today. It sticks
by itself. How do you like it?”
</p>

<p>
“It looks wonderful,” Laura said. So did Marcie, her
cheeks pink with enthusiasm.
</p>

<p>
“You’ve had it, Mann,” Burr said, and Laura heard a
checker smacking triumphantly over the board in a devastating
series of jumps. “Touché, boy.”
</p>

<p>
“Why don’t you take up tiddly winks? I could beat you
at tiddly winks.” Jack sat with his elbows on his knees
and his chin in his hand. He looked up at Laura without
raising his head and smiled. She looked at him, absorbed
in the idea that he had once been infatuated with the
man beside him, and Burr had never known it. Burr
thought he was as normal as himself. But of course,
<a class="pagenum" title="64" id="page-64"></a>
they had never roomed together. Suddenly Laura recalled
that she had agreed to go out with Jack.
</p>

<p>
“Jack—” she began, but he cut her off.
</p>

<p>
“I see I have less allure than your typewriter,” he said.
He cocked an eye at her. “Well, never mind, I don’t have
so many friends I can afford to be jealous of their typewriters.”
</p>

<p>
“Thanks, Jack,” she said with a little smile. She turned
to go into the kitchen, but he jumped up and followed
her.
</p>

<p>
“Where the hell do you think you’re going?”
</p>

<p>
“Straight to the icebox. I’m starved.”
</p>

<p>
“We have a date. I’m taking you out to dinner.”
</p>

<p>
“Why don’t we just stay here?” she pleaded.
</p>

<p>
But all he said was, “No,” and she understood that he
had made his mind up and had something planned.
</p>

<p>
She was reluctant to leave Marcie, who looked so pretty.
But the prettier Marcie looked, the worse Laura suffered.
Maybe it would be better just to talk about her tonight.
Talk to Jack about her. It sounded good.
</p>

<p>
“Okay, but let’s get home early. I’m beat, I really am,”
she said.
</p>

<p>
“Whatever you say, Mother.” He smiled, and she felt
suddenly that it was terribly good to have him for a friend.
</p>

<p>
When they got outside she said, “Let’s go over to Hempel’s.
It’s only a block.”
</p>

<p>
“No. We’re going to The Cellar.”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, God no! It’s miles away. We wouldn’t get home
till midnight.”
</p>

<p>
“A friend of mine wants to meet you.”
</p>

<p>
“Who?”
</p>

<p>
“The name wouldn’t mean anything. It would just
scare you away, probably. She saw you when we were
there last week. She likes your face.”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, that’s ridiculous. Come on, I’m starving. I’ve got
to eat or I’ll faint.”
</p>

<p>
“This is a very interesting girl. She could teach you
a lot.”
</p>

<p>
“I know everything I want to know.” He laughed, but
she went on, “Jack, I’m not going to the Village with you.”
</p>

<p>
But when they reached Broadway he hailed a cab and
she let him put her in it, as she knew she would. “I can’t.
<a class="pagenum" title="65" id="page-65"></a>
I won’t. I’m tired and hungry,” she said. But she got in.
“I’ll fall asleep over my typewriter tomorrow,” she
moaned.
</p>

<p>
When they reached the Cellar she felt a lift of excitement
in spite of herself. They arrived after the kitchen
had closed, but Jack was a regular customer, and they
were willing to fix him up.
</p>

<p>
They followed a waitress to a table. Laura walked with a
strange light queasiness in her stomach and sat down with
Jack feeling terribly self-conscious and looked-at, as if
every pair of eyes in the room was inspecting her. Jack
laughed, waving at somebody. “All my friends’ll think I’ve
gone straight,” he said. He gave the waitress an order
and she scuttled off. He leaned back in his chair to look
at Laura then. “Sorry you’re here?” he asked.
</p>

<p>
“No. But I wish it was Friday night.”
</p>

<p>
“Relax. We’ll leave when you say the word.”
</p>

<p>
She began to feel adventurous and crazy. Jack went up
to get them both a drink. She eyed it with suspicion, but
then she picked it up and drank half of it down, and it
hit her like a bomb, a big soft lovely explosion of warmth
in the pit of her stomach. She blinked at Jack, who only
smiled, knowing the feeling.
</p>

<p>
“How would you like to be in here some night,” he said
slowly, “with Marcie beside you? And sit alone together
at that little table over there? And tell her you love her?”
Laura took another gulp of the drink and almost finished
it. “And hear her say the same thing?”
</p>

<p>
Laura put the glass down with trembling hands. “Oh,
Jack, you bastard,” she said, her insides aflame. “Cut it
out.”
</p>

<p>
“You want it so badly,” he said, “that it’s tearing your
guts out. And it’s never going to happen. So open your
eyes. Look around. There are some beautiful women here
tonight. There’s one as pretty as Marcie.” He squinted
over her shoulder. Laura turned around indignantly to
look, and saw a charming face framed in short brown
curls smiling at a table partner. She looked up at the sudden
sight of Laura’s own face, pale and compelling.
</p>

<p>
“Nobody’s as pretty as Marcie,” Laura told him.
</p>

<p>
“Somebody was,” Jack said, with his peculiar intuition
taking him straight to the point.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="66" id="page-66"></a>
“What do you mean?” Laura said defensively, and finished
her drink.
</p>

<p>
“Whenever you know damn well what I mean,” he said
with a smile, “you ask ‘what do I mean.’ As if I were
nuts. Well, I’m not. Give me your glass.” He took it and
got up. “Never thought you’d beat me to the bottom,
Mother.” He peered into it with one eye and then left to
get it refilled.
</p>

<p>
Laura leaned back in her seat and shut her eyes. After
all, what did it matter if she were here? She felt wonderful.
She had put in a terrific day’s work, she had a right
to a little fling. Her body glowed through its whole length.
Marcie loomed in her mind like a lovely apparition, not
quite real.
</p>

<p>
<em>I’ll have her someday</em>, Laura thought. <em>No matter what
he says.</em>
</p>

<p>
She looked around her, half consciously searching for
someone. But the girl in the black pants wasn’t there. The
crowd was much the same as before, but thinner. The
artist was walking around with his sketch pad, stopping
to talk to tablesful of friends. The bar was crowded, more
than the tables.
</p>

<p>
Jack came back, put a fresh glass in front of her, and
sat down. “Now. What was her name?” he said.
</p>

<p>
Laura opened her eyes slowly. “Who?”
</p>

<p>
“Number one.”
</p>

<p>
She wrinkled her nose in some disgust. “Jack, she wasn’t
a number. Or an animal. Or part of a collection.”
</p>

<p>
“What was she?”
</p>

<p>
“She was a wonderful girl.”
</p>

<p>
“Beautiful like Marcie?”
</p>

<p>
“No. Beautiful—but not like Marcie. They have some
features in common. But Beth was taller. She was quite
boyish.” She felt a little embarrassed suddenly, putting it
this way. “Marcie’s very feminine.”
</p>

<p>
“What are you?”
</p>

<p>
Laura stared at him over the rim of her glass. “What
am I?” she repeated, confusedly. “Do I have to be anything?
I don’t know.”
</p>

<p>
“You’ll find out fast enough,” he said. “Beth probably
taught you a lot. The one who brings you out always
does.”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="67" id="page-67"></a>
“Yes, she did,” she said dreamily. Beth had loosened
her up wonderfully when they were together. She had taken
her by the hand and led her to herself. She had also abandoned
her there. But Laura couldn’t hold anything
against her. That had been a sacred love and always
would be in her memory, like all loves that are broken off
in full passion. If they had been together till it had worn
off a little, Laura might have left her without any desperate
regrets and loneliness. She might have been able to
see Beth as a whole person, not as an ideal. But it hadn’t
happened that way, and Beth still looked like a goddess
to her.
</p>

<p>
Now, in a new world, with new people, she wasn’t sure
what she was. With Marcie she felt aggressive and violent.
Here, in the Cellar, with so many eyes on her, she felt
timid.
</p>

<p>
Jack grinned at her. “You’re a boy,” he said. “With
Marcie, anyway. My friend won’t like that.”
</p>

<p>
Laura put her glass down. “I’m a girl,” she said. “Don’t
look at me that way.”
</p>

<p>
Jack put his head back and laughed. “Correction,” he
said. “You’re a girl. Why don’t you move down here
where you don’t have to be either?”
</p>

<p>
“Everybody has to be one or the other.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re too literal, Laura. Cut off your hair. Wear
those pants you look so nice in. Get some desert boots, a
car coat and some men’s shirts, and you’re in business.”
</p>

<p>
“Jack,” she said, “You are positively revolting.”
</p>

<p>
“That’s the uniform,” he said. “Can’t join the club
without it.”
</p>

<p>
“I don’t want to join.”
</p>

<p>
“Yes you do. You feel good in pants. You swagger.”
</p>

<p>
“I do not!” But she was laughing at him. At herself.
</p>

<p>
“Shhh!” he said softly. “Or they’ll cut you off. Here
comes dinner.”
</p>

<p>
The presence of the waitress made it impossible to talk.
She set a delectable dinner in front of Laura. But somehow,
after the first few bites, it lost its appeal. She sat
gazing at the plate, wondering where her enormous appetite
had gone, pushing a mushroom dreamily from one
side to the other. Jack smiled, watching her. He leaned
over the table on his elbows and picked up her knife.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="68" id="page-68"></a>
“Laura,” he said, pointing at her mushroom. Then he
pushed another one slowly across the plate from the other
side. “Marcie,” he said, nodding at it. The two mushrooms
made contact south of the fried potatoes, and Laura felt
crazy, watching it. It made her smile; she thought it was
ridiculous. It made her want to laugh, and it brought a
warm, unwanted, urgent feeling up in her legs at the same
time. She pushed “Laura” behind the steak.
</p>

<p>
“Ah,” said Jack. “Laura’s afraid of Marcie. But Marcie’s
not afraid of anything. Marcie’s a little heller. Here she
comes.” And he pushed his mushroom after hers. Laura
felt her cheeks get hot.
</p>

<p>
“I’m not going to run away,” she said, and took a large
swallow from her drink, letting “Laura” stay put.
</p>

<p>
“Okay, be a hero,” said Jack. “Make it easy for her.
Look at that little bitch!” and he scooped “Marcie” over
“Laura,” back and forth, the passage facilitated by the
gravy. “She’s nuts. She’s on a kick. She wants you to make
a fool of yourself.”
</p>

<p>
Laura wouldn’t watch. She finished her drink for an
excuse not to look.
</p>

<p>
“But look at Laura,” Jack went on. “She can’t stand it.
Where Marcie goes, Laura goes.” And he pushed “Marcie”
and “Laura” around the plate together.
</p>

<p>
“Stop it, Jack. Get me another drink.”
</p>

<p>
“Here’s where Laura goes crazy.”
</p>

<p>
“Now stop it!”
</p>

<p>
He crammed the two mushrooms into the potatoes, helter
skelter, one over the other. “Laura got what she
wanted,” he said, after a minute, looking up at her
briefly. “But see what happens to her.” And with one
sudden cruel stroke he sliced “Laura” in half. Laura gave
a little start. “‘Marcie’ got bored with the game,” he explained.
</p>

<p>
Laura laughed nervously. “Now cut it out and get me a
drink,” she said.
</p>

<p>
He got up smiling, without a word, and went to the
bar. Laura couldn’t look at the plate. She signaled the
waitress, who came over with a water pitcher.
</p>

<p>
“Will you take this out please?”
</p>

<p>
“Something wrong with it?”
</p>

<p>
“I can’t eat mushrooms.”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="69" id="page-69"></a>
“Well, why dincha say so!” She took the plate with an
angry “Jeez!” muttered under her breath.
</p>

<p>
Jack came back to find her laughing. “Couldn’t take
it, hm?” He nodded at the vacant space where the plate
was, and then looked at Laura. “Want to leave?”
</p>

<p>
“No. I don’t want to go anywhere, Jack. Let’s just sit
here a while and talk.”
</p>

<p>
“About Marcie?”
</p>

<p>
“About Marcie.” She laughed again.
</p>

<p>
“By Jesus, you’re a pretty girl,” he said quietly. “I
didn’t realize it till now. You ought to get soused more
often.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m not soused, I’m in love.”
</p>

<p>
He gave a snort of disbelief. “Okay not-soused and in-love.
You’re headed straight blind for misery. You know
that.”
</p>

<p>
“My eyes are wide open. She’ll never love me.”
</p>

<p>
“Don’t tell me. Tell yourself. Believe yourself.”
</p>

<p>
“I do.”
</p>

<p>
He shook his head and laughed a little. “I see it coming
and I tell you ‘Look out, she’s murder’ and you say ‘You’re
absolutely right’ and then off you go to slit your own
throat.” He leaned over the table seriously. “Leave her,
Laura,” he pleaded, and took her by surprise with his
earnestness. “It’s no good falling for a straight one.
Believe me.”
</p>

<p>
“I won’t leave her,” she said stubbornly. “I know what
I’m doing.”
</p>

<p>
He leaned back with a sigh. “Then at least look at
somebody else,” he said. “Look at Beebo. She’s cruising
you like mad.”
</p>

<p>
“Who’s Beebo? I wouldn’t look at anybody with such a
ridiculous name. What’s cruising?”
</p>

<p>
“Beebo’s a friend of mine. And cruising—well—you’ll
catch on.” He grinned.
</p>

<p>
Laura turned warily around. At the bar sat the handsome
boyish girl she had admired the week before. She
was gazing boldly, but without great interest, at Laura.
When Laura turned to see her she smiled, very slightly.
</p>

<p>
Laura turned back to Jack. “Is that Beebo?” she asked.
“In the black pants?”
</p>

<p>
Jack laughed at her. “You mean tan shorts?”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="70" id="page-70"></a>
Laura looked again. “Well, she had on black pants last
time.”
</p>

<p>
“Did she?” He grinned. “She says you had on a blue
dress with a white collar. You did, too. I remember it.
She liked it.”
</p>

<p>
Laura stared at him and then got indignant. “What’s she
doing, remembering my clothes like that? That’s silly.”
</p>

<p>
“So are you. You noticed hers.”
</p>

<p>
“I just—oh, damn! She followed us into the john.”
</p>

<p>
“I know. She talked to me before she went after you.
I told you, Mother. She likes your face.”
</p>

<p>
“She likes Marcie’s. That’s why she followed us,” she
snapped.
</p>

<p>
He shook his head “no.”
</p>

<p>
“How do you know?” Laura flared, the jealousy working
in her.
</p>

<p>
“I know Beebo,” he chuckled.
</p>

<p>
Laura was getting curious. She finished her drink in
three big swallows, which made Jack laugh. “Is she a friend
of yours?” she said.
</p>

<p>
He shrugged. “More or less. I keep running into her at
parties. For years we ran into each other before we finally
got acquainted. I like her. She’s a hellion, but I like her.
She’s a cynic like me.”
</p>

<p>
“What a pity.” Laura feigned unconcern, running a
wet finger around the edge of her glass. “She looks like
Beth,” she said. “A little.”
</p>

<p>
Jack blew smoke through his nostrils from a freshly
lighted cigarette. “That means you like her,” he said.
</p>

<p>
Laura refused to honor such nonsense with an answer.
She was rather drunk now. She turned again to look at
Beebo. Beebo was still gazing at her, and she winked,
with that faint private smile still on her face. Laura
turned quickly back to Jack. “Is she coming over?” she
said, feeling slightly elated.
</p>

<p>
Jack was grinning past her at Beebo and nodding. At
her words he glanced at her. “No,” he said. “She’s an
uppity bitch.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was disappointed.
</p>

<p>
“Another drink?”
</p>

<p>
“One more. That’s all. What time is it?”
</p>

<p>
“Eleven-thirty.”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="71" id="page-71"></a>
“No!” She tried to collect her thoughts, to right her
time sense, while Jack fetched the drinks. When he came
back she said, “How many drinks have I had?”
</p>

<p>
“Jesus, Mother, what a thing to ask a man. I can’t
even keep track of my own.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m lost,” she said. “I’ve lost count.”
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
“Shall we take off, Mother mine?” Jack said, very
carefully.
</p>

<p>
Laura tried to clear her head by shaking it and pressing
her eyes shut. “I guess we’d better,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“I guess we <em>have</em> to. It’s four o’clock. They’re closing.”
</p>

<p>
“Four!” Laura came half awake at this.
</p>

<p>
“Four o’clock,” he repeated elaborately.
</p>

<p>
“Oh, God. Oh, my head.”
</p>

<p>
“Never mind, Mother, you can stay with me tonight.
I’ll try to keep my hands off you.” He laughed to himself.
</p>

<p>
Laura saw Jack looking up at somebody with a grin
and heard him say, “Hi, doll. I want you to meet my
mother. Mother, look alive.” He squinted at her doubtfully.
“If possible,” he added.
</p>

<p>
Laura looked up and saw a startlingly handsome face
gazing down at her: black hair, pure blue eyes, a slight
smile that widened a little when Laura turned her face up.
</p>

<p>
“Hello,” Beebo said. “Laura.” Her smile gave emphasis
to the way she said Laura’s name.
</p>

<p>
Laura put her hands to her head dizzily. “You look
just like Beth,” she murmured.
</p>

<p>
At which Beebo grinned, turning to Jack. “Three aspirins
and some warm tomato juice,” she said. “First thing when
she gets up. She’ll live.”
</p>

<p>
Laura watched her, fascinated, half smiling.
</p>

<p>
Beebo turned back to her and returned the smile. Then
she reached into her pocket and pulled out a dime. She
flipped it in the air and then dropped it insolently in front
of Laura. “Here’s a dime, sweetheart,” she said. “Call
me sometime.” And with a little grin at Jack, she turned
and left them.
</p>

<p>
Laura stuck her chin out indignantly. She was not too
drunk to be insulted. “Well, thanks a bunch, your majesty!”
she said sarcastically to Beebo’s back. She could
<a class="pagenum" title="72" id="page-72"></a>
hear Beebo laughing but she wouldn’t turn around. She
was already headed for the door.
</p>

<p>
Laura let Jack drag her to his apartment, three blocks
away. He took her up a few stone steps into a long dim
hall, and opened the first door on the left. He steered
her to his bed and pushed her till she collapsed backwards
on it. She fell asleep at once. Jack pulled her shoes
off and her skirt, with total unconcern for her femininity,
and got her under the covers.
</p>

<p>
Laura slept like a stone, a deep almost motionless sleep
that could have lasted far into the next day. But Jack
got her up at seven-thirty. She had three and a half hours
sleep, on virtually no dinner and eight or ten stiff drinks.
She felt strange new pains all over. Jack was used to such
excesses, though he tried to ration himself to one or two a
week. He took it pretty well in stride, but Laura felt
awful. Her first words when Jack shook her were, “Oh,
God! What time is it?”
</p>

<p>
“Seven-thirty.”
</p>

<p>
She turned over on her stomach and put her head
down on the pillow. “Where’s Marcie?”
</p>

<p>
“At home. Where else?”
</p>

<p>
“What time is it? Oh, I asked you that. My head
hurts.”
</p>

<p>
“Take these, Mother,” he said, handing her some aspirin
and a glass of water.
</p>

<p>
“I don’t think I can swallow.”
</p>

<p>
“That’s a chance you’ll have to take. Here we go.” He
popped the pills into her mouth and gave her the water.
She gulped them convulsively. “That’ll see you through
till—” He looked at his watch. “—about noon. After that,
take three more and a No-Doz tablet. And hit the sack
tonight about six. It’s Friday. You can sleep for two days.”
</p>

<p>
“I will, too.” She rolled gingerly to a sitting position,
and looked at Jack with aching eyes. “You did this to
me,” she said mournfully.
</p>

<p>
“Be fair, Mother. I said I’d go whenever you wanted to.
I kept asking and you kept saying no.”
</p>

<p>
She stared at him, disbelieving. “Jack, you louse. You
should have dragged me out, you knew I—what’s that?”
There was a dime on the bed table.
</p>

<p>
Jack grinned at her. “Beebo’s calling card,” he said.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="73" id="page-73"></a>
Laura remembered it in a flash, although the rest of the
evening was little more than a blur. She picked it up and
threw it angrily across the room. “Give it back to her for
me,” she said. “All I remember about last night is that
awful girl and those awful mushrooms! God!”
</p>

<p>
Jack went out of the room laughing.
</p>

<div class="chapter">

<h2 class="chapter" id="chapter-0-8">
<a class="pagenum" title="74" id="page-74"></a>
Chapter Eight
</h2>

</div>

<p class="first">
<span class="firstchar">T</span><span class="postfirstchar">he</span> first thing Laura did when she got to work was to
call Marcie.
</p>

<p>
“Where were you?” Marcie demanded. “I was just going
to call you. I was worried sick.”
</p>

<p>
“You were?” She felt a momentary relief from her headache.
</p>

<p>
“Are you all right?”
</p>

<p>
“I think so. It got so late. We were talking. I finally
spent the night—” It suddenly occurred to her, as if a
brick had dropped on her tender head, that she had spent
the night in a man’s apartment. For the first time in her
life. Never mind that it was an innocent stay, or a short
one. Or that the man had no designs on her. It was the
idea of the thing.
</p>

<p>
“I spent the night with Jack,” she blurted. Marcie was
silent, having suspected as much, but not sure what to
say. And it was then Laura realized that she had said the
best possible thing. Even if it hadn’t happened it would
have been the best thing to say. Marcie didn’t know either
of them was gay. She only knew they were man and girl
and they had spent a night together. What could sound
more normal, more straight? Immoral, maybe, slightly immoral.
But straight.
</p>

<p>
Marcie laughed finally.
</p>

<p>
“Is it funny?” Laura said.
</p>

<p>
“I’m sorry,” Marcie said through her giggles. “I never
dreamed you and Jack would hit it off like this. He must
really like you, Laur.” She sobered suddenly. “He never
took much to the other girls we fixed him up with.”
</p>

<p>
Laura squirmed a little.
</p>

<p>
“But he talked you up for half an hour before you got
home last night. He thinks you’re very pretty.”
</p>

<p>
Laura felt grateful to him. He must have done it to
enhance her in Marcie’s eyes, even if he did disapprove of
her infatuation.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="75" id="page-75"></a>
“Did he say that?” she said, pleased.
</p>

<p>
“He did. And he’s right.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was taken aback. Then she said quickly, “You’re
both crazy.”
</p>

<p>
“No, you are. We’re right. You never looked at yourself,
you silly girl. You don’t <em>know</em> what you look like.”
</p>

<p>
“Do you?” It sounded stupid, but it came out in spite
of her.
</p>

<p>
“Sure. I’ve looked at you when you weren’t noticing.”
</p>

<p>
<em>When could that have been?</em>
</p>

<p>
“I think you have a fascinating face.”
</p>

<p>
“But not pretty.”
</p>

<p>
“Lovely.”
</p>

<p>
“Marcie, listen to me, I—” She was shaky, mixed up
from the hangover and the unexpected flattery. She should
have said something terrible, something intimate, if Dr.
Hollingsworth hadn’t come in early. He nodded at her as
he walked through the office. Sarah was still in the wash
room putting on her face.
</p>

<p>
“’Morning, my dear,” said the doctor with a sort of
modified bow as he sailed past.
</p>

<p>
She returned the greeting and Marcie said, “I’ll get you
in dutch. You’d better hang up. Will you be home tonight?”
</p>

<p>
“Yes. I’ll be home.”
</p>

<p>
“Good girl. See you at six.”
</p>

<p>
Laura hung up bewildered. She felt good and she felt
lousy. Her head was throbbing but her heart was high.
She wanted to talk to Jack. She didn’t care who caught
her. She picked up the phone and dialed his office.
</p>

<p>
“I just talked to Marcie,” she said, unable to keep
the pleasure out of her voice.
</p>

<p>
He sensed her excitement. “Did she propose?” he
said, wryly amused.
</p>

<p>
“No, you idiot!” Laura burst out laughing. “She
said I was beautiful.” Unconsciously she exaggerated.
</p>

<p>
“Jesus, she has a screw loose,” he said.
</p>

<p>
“She said you were talking about me before I got
home. Thanks, Jack.”
</p>

<p>
“Listen, Mother,” he said. “Let’s get this straight.
I want her to think we’re nuts for each other. I also think
you’re a pretty girl, and I said so. But I didn’t say
it to get Marcie all steamed up.”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="76" id="page-76"></a>
“Well, I don’t care why you did it. She is steamed
up.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re making a mountain out of a molehill.”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, Jack, be nice to me! I’m in love, for God’s sake!”
</p>

<p>
“Okay, you’re in love. I believe you. Worse things
have happened. I’ll have to work a drastic cure on you.”
</p>

<p>
She laughed at him. “Too late,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“You’re not going to get stabbed like I was, Laura.”
</p>

<p>
She recalled the defenseless mushroom with a little
shiver of distaste. “Don’t be morbid,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“I’m a realist.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re blind. She’s falling for me. I can tell. She
must be, or she wouldn’t—” Here Sarah walked in. “Jack,
I’ll call you back,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“I dare you to. Call me back this afternoon, when
you’re hung over to your knees, and tell me how much
you’re in love.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m telling you now!”
</p>

<p>
“You talk like a fish. Go on, type your damn reports.
Send some poor bastard up for TB. Or enlarged heart.
At least it’ll be a normal disease.”
</p>

<p>
She hung up with a smack. <em>He’s a morbid miserable
old man</em>, she thought. <em>I’m not that cynical yet.</em>
</p>

<p>
“Was that your friend?” Sarah said. “Jack?”
</p>

<p>
“Yes.” She put paper and carbons into her machine.
</p>

<p>
“He’s giving you the rush, hm?”
</p>

<p>
“I guess so.”
</p>

<p>
“That’s one of us, anyway.”
</p>

<p>
Laura looked at her, and caught an expression of
frustration on her face that made Laura’s problems seem
smaller. Sarah was plain. She was unremarkable. But
so nice. It was depressing. Laura put a hand on her
arm. It would cheer her up if she could cheer someone
else. “Maybe Jack could fix you up with a friend of
his,” she said. “We could make it a foursome.”
</p>

<p>
Sarah shook her head. “They all want to know ‘Is she
pretty?’ Never mind if she’s nice or wants a man so
badly she could ... excuse me, Laura, I sound like an
old maid already. I haven’t given up yet.”
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
Laura studied her on the way down in the elevator.
She ached to say the things she thought, but she didn’t
know Sarah well enough. They were walled up in themselves.
<a class="pagenum" title="77" id="page-77"></a>
<em>Poor plain Sarah</em>, she thought. <em>I’m not beautiful
either. But I’ve been loved. I know love and I can tell
you, you don’t have to be a beauty to feel passion. Sometimes
it helps if you’re not. I wonder if you know that
already.</em>
</p>

<p>
“I’ll talk to Jack,” she said as they walked out.
</p>

<p>
“That’d be awfully nice, Laura.” She laughed diffidently.
“At twenty-eight you begin to feel kind of frantic,”
she said.
</p>

<p>
Outside she left Sarah and walked toward the subway
station. All the way she noticed the women, as she never
had before. She was at a loss to explain it. Before, she
had always hurried, on her way somewhere, with a
deadline to beat, somebody to meet, things on her mind.
Now—perhaps it was the fatigue that made her slow—she
sauntered, looking at the women.
</p>

<p>
Looking at their faces: sweet, fine-featured, delicate,
some of them; others coarse, sensual, heavily female.
They all appealed to her, with their soft skirts,
their clicking heels, their floating hair. It caught
in her throat, this aberration of hers, in a way it
never had until that moment. It suffused her. She surrendered
quietly to her feelings, walking slowly, looking
without staring but with a warm pleasure that
made her want to smile. She had trouble controlling
her mouth.
</p>

<p>
<em>God, I love them</em>, she thought to herself, vaguely surprised.
<em>I just love them. I love them all. I know I’m nuts,
but I love them.</em> She stopped by a jewelry window where
an exquisite girl was admiring a group of rings.
She was all in gray, as fine and soft as twilight. Gray
silk graced her slim legs, gray suede pumps with the
highest heels were on her feet. A gray suit, impeccably
tailored, terribly expensive—gray gloves—a tiny gray
hat. Laura had never liked gray much before, but suddenly
it was ideal on this cool dainty little creature,
with her small nose and moist pink lips. She was extremely
pretty. She looked up to find Laura gazing at
her, collected herself with pretty confusion, and went
off, pulling a recalcitrant gray poodle after her. Laura
had not even noticed it till it moved. She looked after
the girl for a minute with a foolish smile.
</p>

<p>
When she finally reached the subway she collapsed
<a class="pagenum" title="78" id="page-78"></a>
on a seat, exhausted. She wanted to get home and into
bed so badly that she could hardly wait.
</p>

<p>
She was late getting home but even so, Marcie had
not arrived. She wanted very much to see her, but there
was no help for it. She would have to wait. She fell on
her bed, meaning to rest for a minute before she took
her bath. But so tired was she that before five minutes
had passed she was asleep. She woke up to hear small
sounds in the bedroom, and it seemed like perhaps
half an hour had passed.
</p>

<p>
She opened her eyes and found herself all tangled up in
her clothes, her shoes still on, her dress wrinkled. There
was a light on, the small table lamp between the beds.
She pulled herself up and turned around. Marcie was
standing in the bathroom door, with a frame of light
around her, holding her toothbrush and smiling at
Laura. She was all in white lace, in a short gown that
barely reached her thighs. Laura smiled at her and
blinked, shaking her head slightly.
</p>

<p>
“Know what time it is?” Marcie said.
</p>

<p>
“About seven.”
</p>

<p>
“Quarter of twelve.” Marcie laughed at her surprise.
She walked over to her bed and stood beside her for a moment.
She smelled gorgeous—intoxicating, sweet and
clean, faintly powdered, warm and damp from her bath.
She looked sleepy, soft, very feminine. Laura began to
tremble, desperate to touch her, afraid even to look.
</p>

<p>
“You must have been awfully tired, Laur. You’ve been
asleep for hours.”
</p>

<p>
“I could sleep till Monday and never wake up,” Laura
murmured. She spoke without looking at Marcie. She
couldn’t. The scent of her was trouble enough.
</p>

<p>
“Burr and I went out for dinner. We didn’t want to
bother you.”
</p>

<p>
“Did you have a nice big fight?” Irresistibly Laura’s
eyes traveled up Marcie to her face.
</p>

<p>
Marcie sighed. “We always have a nice big fight.”
</p>

<p>
“You must enjoy them.”
</p>

<p>
Marcie sat down beside her. “Don’t talk that way,
Laur,” she said. “I wish I could get interested in books, like
you.”
</p>

<p>
Laura smiled at her, so close, so distant.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="79" id="page-79"></a>
“Help me, Laura,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“How?” Laura felt herself on very shaky ground.
</p>

<p>
“I don’t know how,” Marcie said impatiently. “If
I knew I could help myself. There must be something in
life besides fights, Laur.”
</p>

<p>
“Don’t call me Laur.”
</p>

<p>
Marcie looked at her in surprise. “Why not?”
</p>

<p>
“Somebody else used to call me that. It still hurts a
little.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m sorry. I remember, you told me about him.”
</p>

<p>
Laura felt confession working itself urgently into
her thoughts. She wanted to clasp Marcie to her and
say, “Not ‘him’. Her. <em>Her.</em> It was a girl I loved. As I
love you.” <em>No, not as I love you. I can’t love you that
way, not even you.</em> To her sudden disgust the face of a
handsome arrogant girl named Beebo came up in her
mind. She frowned at her, trying not to see.
</p>

<p>
“What’s the matter, Laur? Laura?” Marcie smoothed
Laura’s hair off her hot forehead. “You must have
loved him a lot.”
</p>

<p>
In a sudden convulsion of desire, Laura threw her
arms around Marcie, pressing her hard, tight, in her
arms. Her need was terrible, and a sort of sob, half ache
and half passion, came out of her. Marcie was frightened.
</p>

<p>
“Laura!” she said, pushing at her. Laura was always
so docile; now suddenly she was strange and violent.
“Laura, are you all right?” Laura only clung to her
the harder, wrestling against herself with all her
strength.
</p>

<p>
For a moment, Marcie tried to calm her, whispering
soothingly and rubbing her back a little. But this
only aggravated Laura.
</p>

<p>
“Marcie, don’t!” she said sharply. Panic began to
well up in her. “Oh, God!” she cried, and stood up
abruptly, shaking all over. She covered her face with
her hands, trying to force the tears back with them.
Marcie watched her, astonished, from the bed.
</p>

<p>
With a little gasp Laura turned and ran out. Marcie
rose to her feet and called after her, but it was too
late. She heard the front door slam as she ran toward it.
She pulled it open but Laura was in the elevator a floor
<a class="pagenum" title="80" id="page-80"></a>
below her and on her way out. Marcie stared into the
black stairwell, feeling shocked and confused.
</p>

<p>
She slipped back into the apartment and into her
bed, but she couldn’t sleep. She simply sat there, her
eyes wide and staring, oscillating between a fear of
something she couldn’t name and bewildered sympathy
for Laura. For whatever it was that tortured her. She
shivered every time she thought of Laura’s near-hysterical
embrace, returning to it again and again. It gave
her a reckless kick, a hint of shameless fun, like the
night she kissed the bum in the park. She didn’t know
why it recalled that to her mind. But it did. Laura
had scared her; yet now she felt like giggling.
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
Laura ran all the way to the subway station, three
blocks off. She fell into a seat gasping, trembling violently.
People stared at her but she ignored them, covering
her face with her hands and sobbing quietly.
She rode down to the Village and got off at Tenth
Street. She had managed to control herself by this time,
but she felt bewildered and lost, as if she didn’t quite
know what she was doing there. She stood for a moment
on the platform, shivering with the chilly air. It was
nearly the end of April, but it was still cold at night.
She had run out in nothing but a blouse and skirt,
with a light topper over them—the clothes she had
fallen asleep in. She was aware of the cold, yet somehow
didn’t feel it.
</p>

<p>
Resolutely she began to walk, climbing the stairs
and then starting down Seventh Avenue. She walked
as if she had a goal, precisely because she had none and
it frightened her. It was Friday night, and busy. People
were everywhere. Young men turned to stare at her.
</p>

<p>
Within five minutes she was standing in front of
The Cellar, rather surprised at herself for having found
it so quickly. There was a strange tingling up and
down her back and her eyes began to shine with a feverish
luster. She walked down the steps and pulled the
door open.
</p>

<p>
Almost nobody noticed her. It was too crowded, at
this peak hour of one of the best nights of the week.
She made her way through the crowd to the nearest end
<a class="pagenum" title="81" id="page-81"></a>
of the bar. She had to squeeze into a corner next to the
juke box and it was work to get her jacket off. It was
sweaty and close after the chill air outside.
</p>

<p>
Laura stood quietly in her corner, looking at all the
faces strung down the bar like beads on a necklace. They
were animated, young for the most part, attractive ...
There were a few that were sad, or old, or soured on life—or
all three. Across the room the artist, with his
sketch pad, was drinking with some friends.
</p>

<p>
Laura felt alone and apart from them all somehow.
There were one or two faces she might have recognized
from the night before, people Jack might have introduced
her to, but she couldn’t be sure. She had been too
drunk to be sure of anything last night.
</p>

<p>
<em>God, was it only last night?</em> she wondered. It seemed
like a thousand nights ago. She didn’t really want to
be noticed now. She only wanted to watch, to be absorbed
in these gay faces, in the idioms, the milieu.
</p>

<p>
“What’ll you have?” She realized the bartender was
leaning stiff-armed on the bar, looking at her.
</p>

<p>
“Whisky and water,” she said, wondering suddenly
how much it would be. She pulled out a dollar and put
it on the counter self-consciously. When he brought
her the drink she gulped it anxiously. Marcie kept
coming into her thoughts; Marcie’s face, her shocked
voice saying, “Laura—don’t!”
</p>

<p>
The bartender took her dollar and brought some
change. It meant she could have another drink. Drinking
your dinner. Where had she heard that? One of
her father’s friends, no doubt. She gazed at the ceiling.
She wanted to talk to Jack, but she was ashamed to call
him. She thought of her father again, and it gave her
a sort of bitter satisfaction to imagine his face if
