her dresser, legs crossed at the ankles, arms folded on her
chest—a favorite stance with her.
</p>

<p>
Laura was suddenly ashamed of the way she had used
Beebo. Beebo was hurt. And it was Laura’s fault.
</p>

<p>
“Everything’s my fault, Beebo,” she said. “I’m sorry.”
There was silence for a minute. Laura was acutely aware
that “I’m sorry” was no recompense for what she was
doing to Beebo.
</p>

<p>
Beebo smiled wryly. “Thanks,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“I am, Beebo. Really. I didn’t come to you last night
just because of Marcie.” It was suffocatingly hard to
talk. She spoke in fits and starts as her nerve came and
left her.
</p>

<p>
“No?” Beebo remained motionless with a ‘tell-me-another’
look on her face.
</p>

<p>
“No. I came—I came because—” She covered her face
with her hands, stuck for words and ashamed.
</p>

<p>
“You came, baby. That’s enough,” Beebo finished
for her, relenting a little. “You came and I’m not sorry.
Neither are you, not really. The situation isn’t perfect.”
<a class="pagenum" title="101" id="page-101"></a>
She laughed. “But last night was perfect. It isn’t
like that very often, I can tell you.”
</p>

<p>
Laura looked at her again. Then she moved toward
her clothes, afraid to stay naked any longer, afraid the
whole thing would start over again.
</p>

<p>
Beebo came toward her, pulling the slip from her
hand and dropping it on the floor. “There’s no hurry,”
she said.
</p>

<p>
“I’m going, Beebo. Don’t try to stop me.”
</p>

<p>
For a moment Beebo didn’t answer. Then she scooped
up some of Laura’s clothes on her foot and flung them at
her. “Okay, baby,” she said. “But next time, you don’t
get off so easily. Clear?”
</p>

<p>
“There won’t be a next time.” Laura concentrated
on dressing, on getting her body covered as quickly as
possible. “I’m grateful to you, but I’ll never do it again.
It isn’t fair, not to you.”
</p>

<p>
Beebo laughed disagreeably. “Don’t worry about being
fair to <em>me</em>, baby. It didn’t bother you last night.”
</p>

<p>
“I couldn’t think last night! You know that.”
</p>

<p>
“Yes. I know that. I’m glad. I hope I drive you out of
your mind.” Beebo’s eyes bored into her and made her
rush and stumble. She was afraid to confront her, and
when she had her clothes on she caught her jacket up
with one hand and headed for the door without looking
back.
</p>

<p>
Nix pranced after her. Before she got the front door
open, Beebo caught her and turned her around. “Goodby,
Beebo,” she said stiffly.
</p>

<p>
Beebo smiled, upsetting Laura with her nude closeness.
“You’ll be back, Little Bo-peep. You know that,
don’t you.” It was a statement, not a question.
</p>

<p>
<em>I’ll never come back</em>, she told herself. <em>I’ll never open
this door again.</em>
</p>

<p>
And, confident that she meant what she said, she turned
and walked away. Within minutes she was riding uptown
on the subway. In less than half an hour she was climbing
the flight of stairs to the penthouse, her heart pounding.
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
The door was open. Laura went in, feeling her legs start
to shake. There didn’t seem to be anyone around. She
walked through the apartment: no-one. She slipped her
<a class="pagenum" title="102" id="page-102"></a>
jacket off and went into the kitchen to find something
for breakfast. Out on the roof she could hear people laughing,
while competing portable radios squeaked from different
corners. The population of the apartment building
had taken to sunning itself on the roof on fair weekends.
</p>

<p>
Laura ate some toast and orange juice, sitting quietly,
on the kitchen table. <em>She’s out there on the porch. I
know she’s out there</em>, she told herself. She was afraid to
face her, afraid to go looking for her. She wanted to fall
into bed and sleep, but she knew she would never rest
until the thing was straightened out.
</p>

<p>
Laura emptied her orange juice glass and put it down
resolutely on the table. She set her chin and slipped off
the table, heading for the door. She bumped flat into
Jack as he came in.
</p>

<p>
Laura gave a little scream and jumped backwards. “Oh,”
she said, shutting her eyes for a minute to let her heart
come back to normal. “It’s you.”
</p>

<p>
“Say it like you’re glad to see me, Mother,” he said,
smiling wistfully.
</p>

<p>
“I am,” she exclaimed, coming toward him then and
taking his hands. “Oh, Jack, I am. I don’t know what I
would have done—”
</p>

<p>
“Now you’re embarrassing me,” he said. “They’re out
on the roof sunbathing, by the way.”
</p>

<p>
“They?”
</p>

<p>
“Burr’s here.”
</p>

<p>
Laura started for the door, but Jack caught her sleeve.
“Are you cracked?” he said. “This isn’t the time. Wait
till Burr leaves.” Laura stopped, unsure. “You don’t want
to go out there and try to explain it to her now, do you?”
Jack said.
</p>

<p>
“I just want to get it over with.”
</p>

<p>
“It’ll keep. Don’t be pushy.”
</p>

<p>
Laura rubbed her forehead. “You’re right,” she said. “I
can’t talk to her in front of Burr.” She laughed a little. “I
<em>am</em> cracked. People have been telling me that all day.”
</p>

<p>
“You don’t have to say much anyway,” Jack said. “I did
a smooth patch job. She thinks you’re a little goofy. But
harmless.”
</p>

<p>
Laura smiled at him in relief. But as they gazed at
each other the ghost of Beebo came up in her mind and
<a class="pagenum" title="103" id="page-103"></a>
she was suddenly blushing without Jack’s having said a
word. “I—I think I’d better go in and lie down, if you’ll
excuse me,” she said, anxious to get away from him.
</p>

<p>
“Didn’t get much sleep last night?” he said.
</p>

<p>
“Not much.” She looked at the floor, not quite forgiving
him for leaving her in the lurch the night before.
“You sent me home with her,” she reminded him.
</p>

<p>
“I didn’t send you to bed with her. I gave her orders.
I told her no monkey business. She promised to behave.”
He was still smiling, curious to hear her defend herself.
</p>

<p>
Laura wondered quickly whether she could get away
with a lie, ashamed as she was of the truth. But she knew
her hot cheeks would betray her. They always did.
</p>

<p>
“She did behave, Jack.” His eyebrows went up skeptically.
“She—what I mean is—it was me.”
</p>

<p>
He looked at her sideways. “You mean you just sort of
fell into each other’s arms?”
</p>

<p>
“Well, sort of.”
</p>

<p>
“By mistake? In the dark?”
</p>

<p>
“I—I—”
</p>

<p>
“You’re fibbing. I know Beebo. Who made who?” He
opened the ice box and fished out a beer. “Do I have to
say it, or are you going to tell me?”
</p>

<p>
“Well, damn it, who are you?” she exclaimed. “You
have no right to know anything.”
</p>

<p>
“Okay. I’ll get it from Beebo. She says you raped her.”
</p>

<p>
“She’s a liar! I heard her say that. Damn!”
</p>

<p>
Jack laughed, opening the beer. He sniffed it. “God,
what awful stuff,” he remarked. “I only drink it before
noon. Cheers.” He drank, and held the can toward her.
“Want some?”
</p>

<p>
“At this hour?”
</p>

<p>
“Your stomach doesn’t know what time it is.”
</p>

<p>
“<em>Your</em> stomach, maybe.”
</p>

<p>
“Did Beebo jump you, Laura? If she did I’ll break her
head.” He asked it suddenly and quietly, and she saw
that he meant help and comfort to her. He had to stick a
few pins in her, only to pull them out and offer first
aid. It was the way he did things.
</p>

<p>
“She didn’t—no,” Laura said, turning away. “Jack,
don’t make me talk about it.”
</p>

<p>
“You could talk to me before.”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="104" id="page-104"></a>
“You turned me down last night,” she said pettishly.
</p>

<p>
“I had to. There was someone else last night.”
</p>

<p>
“I ... oh damn it, Jack, I’m ashamed of myself. It
was my fault, I made it so easy. No, that’s a lie. I did it
on purpose.” A wave of tears welled through her and subsided,
leaving her with her hands over her face. Jack
started to speak but she silenced him with a wave of her
hand. “It’s been so long, Jack. It’s been hell. I’ve been so
lonely. I didn’t know a person could be that lonely and
live. And then I moved in with Marcie and it was suddenly
torture. All these months I’ve been here. And most
of the time I’ve been dying for her. And last night—I was
so tired, so mixed up and I had a couple of drinks—”
</p>

<p>
“You sounded nuts on the phone.”
</p>

<p>
“I felt nuts. I felt awful. She took me home, and she
was very decent. She really was. She wanted to, I know
that. But she didn’t. She gave me her bed and she went
in the living room and slept on the couch. I thought I’d
fall right to sleep. But I couldn’t. I just tossed and turned,
and every time I heard her turn over I was on fire. It
got too strong for me. I finally gave in. <em>I</em> did it. It was
my fault, Jack.”
</p>

<p>
“Poor Laura.” He said it sympathetically. “Come here,
honey.” He put his arms around her and held her, stroking
her back. When he did it, she didn’t mind. She’d have
resented any other man ... except maybe Merrill Landon.
But Merrill Landon never showed affection to anyone.
</p>

<p>
“I know how it is, believe me,” Jack said. “You’re
starving, and somebody puts a feast in front of you. What
happens after that is Instinct. Overwhelming. You eat. Or
you die of hunger, right there, with all that food in front
of you.”
</p>

<p>
Laura clung to him, letting herself cry softly and gratefully
into his shoulder.
</p>

<p>
“Let me give you just one little word of advice, Mother.
Don’t starve yourself anymore. Or that hunger is going
to kill you.”
</p>

<p>
She looked at him with wet eyes. Her face was strangely
different, and Jack could see it. A night of love, a night
of luxurious satiation, had changed her. For all her fatigue,
her shame at herself, her body was happy and relieved.
She couldn’t help that. She felt physically good,
<a class="pagenum" title="105" id="page-105"></a>
for the first time in over a year, and she had Beebo to
thank for it.
</p>

<p>
“You’re different,” Jack said, smiling. “You look good.
I don’t care how tired you are. It’s becoming—love.”
</p>

<p>
“That wasn’t love.”
</p>

<p>
“What was it, then?”
</p>

<p>
“Just purely physical. Animal. Vulgar.”
</p>

<p>
“Love has a body, Laura. Eyes and lips, legs and sex.
We humans can’t help that.”
</p>

<p>
“Love is bigger and better than that. There hasn’t been
any of that with Marcie and me, but I love her.”
</p>

<p>
“That’s idealistic crap.”
</p>

<p>
Laura gasped, her eyes widening in sudden anger, but
he interrupted her sharp retort before she could make it.
</p>

<p>
“Love is no bigger and better than the people who feel
it,” he said. “What has your love for Marcie got you? A
fat neurosis, a lot of misery, and a night in bed with somebody
you hardly know because you couldn’t stand it any
longer. If that’s what makes it bigger and better, the hell
with it. Feed it to the crocodiles.” And he turned brusquely
away.
</p>

<p>
Laura stared at him, unable to answer him. It struck
her harshly that he might be right. “But I love Marcie,”
she whispered hoarsely.
</p>

<p>
“Sure. You love her because she looks like Beth, or
whatever the hell her name is.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was shocked. “No, no, Jack you don’t understand.
That has nothing to do with it. I love her.” He turned
to look at her, cynicism written plain on his face. “I love
her because—”
</p>

<p>
“Because she’s under the same roof with you, two feet
away when you go to bed at night. Because she’s young
and pretty. Because you can’t have her.”
</p>

<p>
“Because I can’t have her!” she exclaimed contemptuously.
“That’s exactly why I’m so miserable, you idiot!
I love her so much—”
</p>

<p>
“We all do. She’s a great girl,” he said, so vaguely and
quietly, that it calmed her a little.
</p>

<p>
But when Marcie said, “What are you two talking
about?” Laura jumped, visibly startled. “Oh, I didn’t
mean to scare you,” Marcie said. She had made up her
mind to treat Laura gently and carefully. Burr came in
<a class="pagenum" title="106" id="page-106"></a>
noisily behind her. “Hi, Laura,” he said, and stared at
her pale face.
</p>

<p>
“I didn’t hear you coming,” Laura said nervously. She
wondered what Marcie had told Burr, and suddenly it
was too much to stand there and face them. “I’m going
to bed,” she said suddenly, briefly. “I didn’t sleep much
last night.” It came to her then that she didn’t know what
she was supposed to have done last night at all. Jack
hadn’t told her.
</p>

<p>
Jack, faster than Laura when he was on the spot, said,
“Laura spent the night with Beebo Brinker. She’s an old
friend of mine.” He spoke to Burr who apparently didn’t
know what to think.
</p>

<p>
<em>Damn Jack!</em> Laura thought. <em>He didn’t have to say her
name. That ridiculous name!</em>
</p>

<div class="chapter">

<h2 class="chapter" id="chapter-0-9">
<a class="pagenum" title="107" id="page-107"></a>
Chapter Nine
</h2>

</div>

<p class="first">
<span class="firstchar">M</span><span class="postfirstchar">arcie</span> tried to be understanding with Laura when they
were alone later. She said, “Jack told me all about it,
Laura. I understand.”
</p>

<p>
<em>What did he tell you? What do you understand? Why
didn’t he tell me?</em> She didn’t know how to act with
Marcie. Her discomfort made her awkward and for the
first time she found herself wishing to be without her
for a little while. She didn’t want Marcie to try to comfort
her. She just wanted to let it blow over.
</p>

<p>
But Marcie was a warm-natured girl, and she was curious.
She wanted to sit on Laura’s bed and talk about it.
She kept saying, “Tell me about it, Laur. Tell me what
happened. Don’t you know I wouldn’t be shocked?”
</p>

<p>
At this point Laura revolted. “No, I <em>don’t</em> know!” she
said, and was immediately sorry. She raised her hand to
her mouth. “Marcie, please. Please drop it.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m sorry, Laur. I can’t do anything right tonight.”
She looked so disheartened that Laura had to smile at her
a little.
</p>

<p>
“You do everything right, Marcie,” she said soothingly.
“I’m the one who’s wrong. No, it’s true. I’m not like you.
I can’t confess to people.”
</p>

<p>
“You tell Jack things.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was suddenly alert, alarmed. “How do you
know?” she demanded. “What things?”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, you’re always going off and talking. Like this
morning. Why don’t you have long talks with me?”
</p>

<p>
Laura sighed, relieved. “I don’t know. Jack is so easy to
talk to, Marcie.”
</p>

<p>
“Does that mean I’m not? I try to be.” She smiled invitingly.
</p>

<p>
Laura, who had been lying on her bed, raised herself
up on her elbows. “I never say these things like I mean
them,” she apologized. “I only mean, I—” <em>I can’t talk
to you because I’m in love with you, that’s what I mean.
But that’s not what I can say.</em>
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="108" id="page-108"></a>
She rolled over on her stomach and buried her face in
the pillow. Marcie sat motionless for a minute, afraid to
say anything and start her off again. Then she leaned
over her and touched her shoulders. “You don’t have to
tell me, Laura, honey,” she said. “I guess I shouldn’t
pester you. Jack says you’ve been through a lot and that’s
why you’re nervous. I don’t want to make you unhappy,
Laura. I’m afraid I do sometimes. I don’t know why, I
just get the feeling now and then, when you look at me,
that I make you sad. Do I?”
</p>

<p>
Laura’s nails cut into her smooth white forehead.
“Marcie, don’t torture me,” she said. Her voice was low
and strained. It was such an odd thing to say that Marcie
withdrew, and climbed into her own bed.
</p>

<p>
“I’m sorry,” she whispered, pulling the covers up and
turning out the light. Then she put her hands over her
face suddenly and sobbed.
</p>

<p>
“Oh, Marcie!” Laura was out of bed before she had
time to think, sitting next to Marcie and holding her.
“Don’t cry, Marcie. Oh God, why can’t I ever say anything
right?” She implored the ceiling for an answer. “I
didn’t mean to hurt you.”
</p>

<p>
Marcie slowed down and stopped almost as suddenly as
she began. “I know,” she said. “I know what it is. I used
to drive Burr nuts this way, asking questions and talking
and talking. And when he wouldn’t answer, I just kept
asking more and more till I drove him crazy. I don’t
know why. I guess I <em>wanted</em> to drive him crazy. But I
don’t know why I do it to you.” She looked away, embarrassed.
Laura’s arms tightened involuntarily around
her. She had no idea how to answer this unexpected outburst.
She was afraid to try to comfort Marcie, for the
very act of soothing her brought Laura’s own emotions to
a boil. The safest course was to get back in bed at once
and forget it. Or at least, stop talking. But Marcie was
clinging to her and she couldn’t roughly shake her off.
</p>

<p>
“I’ve learned a lot from living with you, Laur,” Marcie
said quietly. Laura listened, her nostrils full of the scent
of flowers. “This may sound silly to you but—don’t take
this wrong, Laura—but I admire you, I really do. You
have a quality of self control that I could never learn.
You keep your thoughts to yourself. If you don’t have
<a class="pagenum" title="109" id="page-109"></a>
anything to say, you don’t <em>say</em> anything. If you don’t want
to talk, you don’t.”
</p>

<p>
She looked up and laughed a little ruefully. “I talk all
the time, as if I had to. Just living with you, I’m beginning
to see it. I talk all the time and say nothing. You
almost never talk, but when you do it’s worth listening to.”
</p>

<p>
Laura began to squirm uncomfortably, but Marcie
grasped her sleeves and continued. “You know something,
Laur? I think I just drove Burr crazy. I talked him to
death.”
</p>

<p>
“He still loves you, Marcie.” Laura found her hand on
Marcie’s hair, without quite knowing how she had let it
happen. “He wants you back.”
</p>

<p>
“I know. We’ve hardly quarreled at all this week, Laur.
You haven’t been around much, you haven’t seen us. But
we’ve been getting along unusually well. But the screwy
part is, it’s not like I thought it would be.”
</p>

<p>
“You mean, you miss the quarrels?”
</p>

<p>
“I mean I just wish he wouldn’t come around so much
any more. I want time to change. To think.”
</p>

<p>
“Think about what?”
</p>

<p>
“About me. No, about anything <em>but</em> me. That’s all I
ever thought about before. You think about other things.
You know what’s going on. You come home at night and
you read all these books that are sitting around. You
can’t even talk to me about them, because you know how
stupid I am.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was astonished. All these critical thoughts had
gone through Marcie’s head, and Laura hadn’t been
aware of it. Marcie had been watching her, admiring her,
and she hadn’t known that either. <em>I’m plumb blind</em>, she
thought. <em>And I thought I couldn’t know Marcie any better.
Because I love her. And she talks like this to me.
God!</em>
</p>

<p>
“Marcie, you don’t need to read books. It’s just a bad
habit for introverts.” Marcie shook her head silently while
Laura went on. “Beautiful girls like you don’t need to
read,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“That’s just it,” Marcie said. “I’m not going to be just
another pretty idiot. I want to know something. I’m sick
of knowing absolutely nothing. I want to be different.
I want you to help me.”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="110" id="page-110"></a>
<em>She wants me</em>, Laura thought happily. <em>She wants me.</em>
It was all she heard.
</p>

<p>
“When you were gone all night with Jack—” She
paused and looked away. “—I started to think.
I couldn’t sleep, I don’t know why. I was thinking about
you, Laur. I was wondering why you never talk to me,
why we have so little to say to each other. We sit at the
breakfast table and read the paper and go off without
anything more than ‘good morning’. At night we go to
bed and sometimes I talk, but it’s not a conversation. You
listen, I guess you listen.”
</p>

<p>
“I do!”
</p>

<p>
“And I say the wrong things. And you go to pieces,
like Friday night.”
</p>

<p>
“No.”
</p>

<p>
“Or else you run away. You go sleep with Jack.”
</p>

<p>
“Marcie!”
</p>

<p>
“I know you were with Jack again last night. He didn’t
have to lie to me about it.”
</p>

<p>
“But he didn’t. I wasn’t!”
</p>

<p>
“Now don’t <em>you</em> lie to me!”
</p>

<p>
Laura stared at her, unable to speak.
</p>

<p>
“Help me, Laura,” Marcie said, leaning toward her. “I
want to change. I’m sick of myself. I’m sick of Burr.”
</p>

<p>
The strangest craziest feeling started up in Laura; just
an echo, faraway in herself. <em>She wants me to help her,
to be with her. She admires me. Dear God, I’m afraid
to wonder how much.</em> A very small smile curved the corners
of her mouth.
</p>

<p>
“I have to start somewhere,” Marcie said. “I want to
talk to you like an intelligent human being, not an
ignoramus.”
</p>

<p>
Laura smiled at her. Almost without her realizing it,
her hand had stolen back to Marcie’s yellow hair. “Do
you, Marcie?” she said. It was a simple question, but it
asked a thousand others.
</p>

<p>
“Yes.”
</p>

<p>
“Why?”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, I’m fed up with myself. I never realized, till I
lived day-in-day-out with you, how much I’d been missing.
Give me a book to read, Laur.”
</p>

<p>
“In the morning.” Laura smiled at her and got up, edging
away from her bed.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="111" id="page-111"></a>
“Now.”
</p>

<p>
“It’s too late, Marcie. You won’t read anything now.”
</p>

<p>
“I want to tell Burr I read a book.”
</p>

<p>
“I’ll give you something later,” Laura said. It sounded
strangely insinuating, the way she said it. She scared herself.
She ducked into her bed as into a safe harbor, and
hid her body under the blankets.
</p>

<p>
With a sigh Marcie turned the light out. After a moment’s
silence she whispered, “Laur? Will you talk to me
after this? Really talk to me? Tell me things?”
</p>

<p>
“I’ll try,” Laura murmured, frowning in the dark. She
lay in bed daydreaming for hours, seeing the first signs
in Marcie of an influence she had been unaware of.
Where would it lead? What doors would it open? Would
it lead them both to bitterness? Or mutual ecstasy?
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
In the morning Laura was very matter-of-fact. She almost
ignored Marcie. She made her work for her attention
and it delighted her that Marcie was willing to work
for it. Instinctively Laura knew she had to play hard to
get, and she liked to play that way for once.
</p>

<p>
At breakfast, after a few false starts, Marcie blurted,
“I’ll be late tonight.” She put her paper down and faced
Laura.
</p>

<p>
Laura looked up slowly. “Date with Burr?” she said.
</p>

<p>
“No. Mr. Marquardt is having some out-of-town guests
for dinner downtown. He asked some of us to go. I told
him I would.”
</p>

<p>
“Have fun,” Laura said, and looked back at the front
page.
</p>

<p>
“Ha! Some drunken idiot of a reporter’ll probably
pester me to death.”
</p>

<p>
“A reporter?” Laura looked up again suddenly.
</p>

<p>
“Oh, I don’t know.” Marcie saw Laura’s interest and it
sparked her own. “A journalist, or something. It’s a convention—professional
fraternity, I guess.”
</p>

<p>
“What fraternity? What’s it called?”
</p>

<p>
“Ummm.” Marcie bit her lip and concentrated. “It’s
Greek. Let’s see. Something the matter?”
</p>

<p>
“No. Is it Chi Delta—”
</p>

<p>
“—Sigma. That’s it, I remember. How did you know?
Now something <em>is</em> the matter, Laura!”
</p>

<p>
Laura had gone very pale. She swallowed convulsively.
<a class="pagenum" title="112" id="page-112"></a>
“I just remembered, I was supposed to run an errand for
Dr. Hollingsworth. I’d better get going.” She got up suddenly
and went into the bedroom for her jacket.
</p>

<p>
Marcie stood up and followed her. “You didn’t finish
your breakfast, Laur,” she said, concerned, a line of worry
in her forehead.
</p>

<p>
“I’m not hungry. I’ll see you tonight,” she said, and
turned quickly to almost run out.
</p>

<p>
Marcie came after her, bewildered. “Laura, you don’t
make sense,” she said. “What’s the matter with you?”
</p>

<p>
But Laura was running down the stairs to the elevator.
Marcie turned and went back into the kitchen and drank
her coffee standing, gazing perplexed at Laura’s plate.
</p>

<div class="chapter">

<h2 class="chapter" id="chapter-0-10">
<a class="pagenum" title="113" id="page-113"></a>
Chapter Ten
</h2>

</div>

<p class="first">
<span class="firstchar">M</span><span class="postfirstchar">errill</span> Landon. <em>Merrill Landon. My father. My father
is coming to New York. He never misses these damn
things, he goes every year. Oh, God, help me.</em> Laura rode
down to work on the subway, her fists clenched in her
lap, her face set like a mask to cover the torment inside.
</p>

<p>
<em>He doesn’t know I’m here, that’s one good thing. He’ll
never find me, either. How long will he be here? It must
be in the papers. I missed it at breakfast.</em>
</p>

<p>
She picked up the <em>Times</em> on the corner where she left
the subway. She took it up to the office with her, impatient
to look at it. Sarah was already there.
</p>

<p>
“Hi, gorgeous,” she said.
</p>

<p>
Laura looked up, startled. “Hi,” she said. “Who’s gorgeous?”
</p>

<p>
“You are. You must be, you’ve got a man.”
</p>

<p>
Laura stared at her blankly, her mind full of the threat
of her father’s presence in the city. Finally it came to her.
“Oh, you mean Jack,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“Did you talk to him?”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, yes. Yes.” <em>Now what the hell does she mean? Why
would I—Oh! I promised her a date.</em> Laura felt suddenly
sunk. All those reports to do that should have been
done before. Lies to tell, at nine in the morning. Merrill
Landon somewhere in New York. It was too much. The
day stretched away in front of her like an endless obstacle
course.
</p>

<p>
“What’d he say?” Sarah said eagerly.
</p>

<p>
“He’s working on it. Maybe this weekend.”
</p>

<p>
“Gee, that sounds great.”
</p>

<p>
Laura had to look at the paper; she <em>had</em> to. It
gnawed at her, as she sat at her desk, sneaking through it
between reports and unable to find anything. Her father’s
name ran through her mind like a robot tune from
a TV commercial.
</p>

<p>
It was a rushed day. Sarah didn’t take a break on days
<a class="pagenum" title="114" id="page-114"></a>
when they were behind, but nothing could have stopped
Laura. She got up and almost ran to the washroom at
eleven, the paper in hand. She felt herself trembling,
going over the pages again and again, until she suddenly
found it at the bottom of page 12. “Chi Delta Sigma, national
journalism fraternity, opens its convention today
at the McAlton Hotel. The convention will last until next
Saturday, at which time ...” etc. There was an agenda
listed, a few names—the national officers. There it was—Merrill
Landon, corresponding secretary. Laura shut her
eyes and groaned a little.
</p>

<p>
The day dragged. She typed until the small round keys
seemed to weigh a pound apiece under her fingers, and
still the reports piled up.
</p>

<p>
Laura sat hunched over her machine for a long time
after the others had left for the day. She meant to work,
but she never did any. She wanted to cry and she couldn’t.
She wanted to move, to talk to someone, to explode, and
she just sat there until the cramps in her back made her
groan. She got up stiffly and put her jacket on and stood
for a moment, aimless and lost. There was nowhere to go,
nothing to do. Marcie wouldn’t be home yet.
</p>

<p>
She rummaged idly in her pockets, pulling out some
change and a shopping list. The list was from the week
before and she started to drop it in the wastebasket, when
she noticed something on the other side. A phone number—Watkins
9-1313. And the initials B. B. Laura crumpled
it in her hand, seized with an uncontrollably pleasant
shudder. Then she threw it indignantly into the wastebasket,
wondering when Beebo had scribbled it out. And
then she leaned over slowly and took it out of the wastebasket
and shoved it furtively back into her pocket, without
looking at it. She sat down abruptly in her chair and
put her head down on her arms and wept.
</p>

<p>
“Father ...” she whispered. “Why did we have to hate
each other? We’re all we have ... Father ...”
</p>

<p>
She got up fifteen minutes later, turned out the lights,
and stole out, quiet as a thief.
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
She walked over to the McAlton Hotel. She had no
idea what she expected to find or to do. But she went
into the big softly carpeted lobby and walked, almost as
<a class="pagenum" title="115" id="page-115"></a>
if she were sleep walking, toward the desk. It was crowded
and noisy, with that ineffable air of excitement that big
hotels seem to generate.
</p>

<p>
Laura felt gooseflesh start up all over her. Many of
these people must be conventioneers. If Merrill Landon
didn’t see her one of his Chicago friends might, and the
secret would be out. He would run her down if it took the
whole New York City police force.
</p>

<p>
She leaned apprehensively on the marble topped
counter of the desk, waiting until a clerk could serve her.
He came up after a couple of minutes, looking enormously
efficient and busy. “May I help you, Miss?” he
demanded.
</p>

<p>
“Is a Mr. Merrill Landon staying here?” she asked.
</p>

<p>
“Just a minute, please.” He disappeared briefly and
Laura looked around the lobby, her hand partially covering
her face.
</p>

<p>
<em>He might see me. I must be out of my mind to come
here.</em> But she waited none-the-less.
</p>

<p>
“He’s in 1402,” the clerk said loudly in her ear.
</p>

<p>
Laura jumped.
</p>

<p>
“Shall I call him?” asked the clerk.
</p>

<p>
“Yes, please.” She had no idea why she was doing this.
She felt as if she were two people, one acting, the other
watching; one compelled to act, the other shocked by the
action.
</p>

<p>
“Who wants to see him, please?”
</p>

<p>
“His daughter.” She almost whispered it, and he made
her repeat it. Then he buzzed off. She watched him, perhaps
ten feet from her but impossible to hear, as he lifted
the receiver, gave the number, waited. Then his face
lighted into a business-type smile, and she saw his lips
form the words, “Mr. Landon?” He went on, and she
watched him, feeling almost sick with anticipation.
</p>

<p>
The clerk came back after a brief conversation. “Well,
Miss—” he began, eyeing her closely.
</p>

<p>
“What did he say?” Laura looked at him with her stark
blue eyes. Her chin trembled.
</p>

<p>
“He says he has no daughter, Miss,” the clerk drawled.
He grinned. “Tough luck. Want to try someone else?”
</p>

<p>
Laura’s mouth dropped open. Her face twitched. She
couldn’t answer him. She turned and ran, bumping into
<a class="pagenum" title="116" id="page-116"></a>
people, stumbling, until she found a phone booth empty
in a row of booths along a far wall and she took refuge
there. She buried her face in her hands and wept. “Merrill
Landon, go to hell, go to hell,” she said fiercely, under
her breath. “I hate you. Oh, God, how I hate you!” And
she sobbed until somebody rapped on the door of the
booth. She wiped her eyes hastily, knowing they were red
and swollen, and turned to glare at the impatient rapper.
He glared back.
</p>

<p>
Defiantly she put a dime in the phone and lifted the
receiver. She called Jack.
</p>

<p>
A voice answered almost at once. A strange masculine
voice. “Hello?” it said.
</p>

<p>
“Jack?” Her voice trembled.
</p>

<p>
“Just a minute.” He called, “Jack, it’s for you.”
</p>

<p>
A few seconds later Jack answered.
</p>

<p>
“Jack, it’s Laura.”
</p>

<p>
“Are you all right?”
</p>

<p>
“I have absolutely nothing to say,” she said. “I’m only
calling because—because I’m in a phone booth and some
fool wants to use the phone. He’s rapping on the door.”
</p>

<p>
“Mother,” he said slowly, “you have a screw loose. Now
listen carefully and do what I tell you. Just go along
quietly and don’t tell them anything. I’ll send my analyst
over right away.”
</p>

<p>
“My father’s in town.” Her breath caught while she
spoke.
</p>

<p>
“Oh! No wonder. Did you tell him to go to hell?”
</p>

<p>
“The desk clerk called his room and said his daughter
wanted to see him.” She stopped to swallow the fury in
her throat.
</p>

<p>
“And he told <em>you</em> to go to hell?”
</p>

<p>
“He said, ‘he had no daughter.’” Her voice trembled
with the immensity of it.
</p>

<p>
Jack, for once, was momentarily speechless. Finally he
said, “He is a bastard, Laura. By God, he is. Don’t mess
with him. Come on over, I’ll buy you a drink.”
</p>

<p>
“Thanks, Jack.” She broke into tears again.
</p>

<p>
“Don’t cry, Laura. Just think what satisfaction that
would give the old s.o.b.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re right!” she said sharply, pulling herself up.
“I won’t. I’ll be right over.”
</p>

<p class="tb">
<a class="pagenum" title="117" id="page-117"></a>
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
Jack was waiting for her on the front stoop, sitting on
one of the cement railings and looking up at what few
stars were available between the roofs. Without a word
he got up, slipped an arm around her, and turned her
away from his apartment. They walked over to a small
bar she hadn’t seen before called Mac’s Alley, without
speaking to each other.
</p>

<p>
The bar was in a basement and you walked down a
flight of twisting stairs to reach it. There were booths
around the walls, tables and a juke box in the middle, and
a long bar ran across the back. Laura walked halfway
towards the bar with Jack before she realized that there
were no other women in the place. She turned to Jack
with anxiety.
</p>

<p>
“Do they want me in here?” she asked.
</p>

<p>
He smiled. “They’re not going to give you a rush,
Mother. I’ll stake my life on it.”
</p>

<p>
“I didn’t think they liked women in a place like this.”
</p>

<p>
Jack guided her to a barstool. “Oh, they’re friendly
enough. They know you wouldn’t be here if you weren’t
gay. They figure, you leave them alone and they’ll leave
you alone.”
</p>

<p>
Laura looked around her uncomfortably. “I can’t help
thinking I embarrass them.”
</p>

<p>
“Maybe they embarrass you. Would you rather go over
to The Cellar?”
</p>

<p>
“No ... I don’t know.”
</p>

<p>
“You don’t want to run into Beebo. She’s usually out
making the rounds about now. That’s why I brought you
here.”
</p>

<p>
Laura smiled gratefully at him. “Thanks,” she said. “I
should have seen it myself.” But she found herself so
shaken by the sudden idea of Beebo loose among scores of
desirable girls that she couldn’t concentrate for a minute.
</p>

<p>
Jack ordered them a drink. Then he turned to her,
pushing his glasses back into place on his nose. They
tended to slide down to the halfway mark. “Well?” he
said, and paused. “Let’s tear Papa Landon apart.”
</p>

<p>
“I don’t want to talk about him,” Laura said.
</p>

<p>
“Then why am I buying you a drink?”
</p>

<p>
She turned to snap at him and then saw he was kidding
her. “Sorry, Jack,” she said. Looking at him brought back
<a class="pagenum" title="118" id="page-118"></a>
her faith in him, and she smiled a little. “I always knew
he was a hard man,” she said softly, “but I never dreamed
he’d go as far as this. I always thought, in spite of everything,
in spite of all the bitterness and misery we’ve had
together, that he must love me a little. After all, I’m all
he has left ... of my mother, my brother ... his family.
I was five when it happened, and I wish to God I could
remember what he was like before. But I can’t. I like to
pretend he was generous and gentle and kind. And I can
remember sitting on his shoulders when we went to a
Fourth of July parade. It was that same summer, before
our vacation. I remember he hoisted me up and bought
me a balloon and held me while the parade went by so I
could see. Afterwards he walked around and talked to his
friends, and he didn’t make me get down. I felt like a
queen on a throne. It’s been my one good memory of him,
to this day. But Mother was with us. Maybe he did it for
her sake.
</p>

<p>
“I remember her better than him from those years.
Sometimes I miss her terribly. She was very loving.”
</p>

<p>
“Maybe,” said Jack, “your father wouldn’t hate you so
now if he hadn’t loved you so much before.”
</p>

<p>
“You give him too much credit,” she said. “After what
he did to me tonight, I’ll never speak to him again. I’d
kill him if I could. But I wouldn’t go near him, even for
that. I wouldn’t give him the satisfaction of seeing my
face. He has no daughter, has he? All right, God damn
him, I have no father! Two can play at that game.”
</p>

<p>
“Don’t hate me for saying it,” Jack said, “but I think
you still love him. I think you’ll see him again.”
</p>

<p>
She turned on him. “You’re crazy!” she said. “You don’t
know anything! What makes you think such a thing?”
</p>

<p>
He shrugged. “Only that it matters so terribly to you.”
</p>

<p>
Laura finished her drink and placed the glass carefully
on the bar, trying to sort out her thoughts. “If I do see
him again,” she said, “it’ll be when I can tell him I’m a
success. Financially. Socially. Every way. I want to tell
him, ‘I have a good job, nice friends. I can get along fine
in this world without you, and I’ll never need you again.’
And you know what else I’d like to say to him, Jack?”
</p>

<p>
“Yeah.” He lit a cigarette. “‘Father, I’m queer. And
it’s all your fault. Shove that up your rear and live with
<a class="pagenum" title="119" id="page-119"></a>
it!’ Yeah, I know. Shock the hell out of him. I tried that
on—on a close relative once.”
</p>

<p>
“What happened?”
</p>

<p>
“I don’t really know. When his face went blue I took
off. I haven’t been home since. I can’t go home to find
out, as a matter of fact. I’m—shall we say—not welcome.”
He said this with slow sarcasm.
</p>

<p>
“Jack, I’m sorry,” she said gently, and looked at him
sympathetically. It occurred to her now, when she found
his own troubles paralleled her own, that he was very
human and not a slick witty party boy without real
feelings. He was lonely. <em>Everybody’s lonely</em>, she thought.
<em>Marcie for a perfect mate. Beebo for a perfect girl.
Jack for an affectionate boy. Me ... Poor Sarah ...</em>
</p>

<p>
That recalled Sarah to her mind. “Jack, I have a
friend,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“Congratulations.”
</p>

<p>
“—named Sarah.”
</p>

<p>
“Does Beebo know?”
</p>

<p>
“And she wants a date.”
</p>

<p>
“With a girl?”
</p>

<p>
“—With a boy. She’s straight.”
</p>

<p>
“What a shame.”
</p>

<p>
“Can you help me out?”
</p>

<p>
“I can help <em>you</em>, Mother, but can I help Sarah?”
</p>

<p>
“You must know somebody. How about that boy who
answered your phone tonight? Could he take her out?”
</p>

<p>
“If he does I’ll break his head for him,” he said and
laughed softly, knocking his cigarette ashes into a
scorched aluminum tray in front of him.
</p>

<p>
“Who is he, Jack?” she asked.
</p>

<p>
“A friend. No, a lover. For the moment, anyway.”
</p>

<p>
Laura put her hand on his arm. “Don’t be so cynical,”
she said.
</p>

<p>
“These things never last.” He shook his head. “Better
to face it at the beginning.”
</p>

<p>
“He must see something worthy in you or he never
would have come to you in the first place.” She spoke
awkwardly, but with sympathy.
</p>

<p>
“He sees dollar bills.” Jack smiled.
</p>

<p>
“Jack! Don’t be so hard on yourself. It hurts me.” She
didn’t like to see him stick the pins in himself. It
<a class="pagenum" title="120" id="page-120"></a>
was all right when he did it to her, because it was in
fun. She didn’t mind, she understood his need. But
when he hurt himself he hurt in dead earnest.
</p>

<p>
“Besides, you aren’t rich,” Laura said. “If that’s all
he wanted, he’d find somebody else.”
</p>

<p>
“I have a little put away,” he said. “I save it up in
between affairs. When somebody irresistible comes
along, I spend it like a fool. Makes a wonderful impression
the first couple of weeks. Then I’m flat broke and
all alone again. My chronic condition.”
</p>

<p>
It was a pathetic revelation. Laura was taken aback
by it. “You shouldn’t do it, Jack,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“I can’t help it. I’ll hang on to him with anything.
Anything I’ve got. Even dollar bills.”
</p>

<p>
“If all he wants is your money he’s not <em>worth</em> your
money! Or your time, or your friendship.”
</p>

<p>
“Laura, this isn’t friendship. This is another subject
entirely. Honest to God.” Laura blushed. “A man
can’t buy a friend. But there’s always a little love for
sale.”
</p>

<p>
“Not real love.”
</p>

<p>
He shrugged. “I don’t ask for the moon.”
</p>

<p>
Laura finished her drink. “What’s his name?” she
said.
</p>

<p>
“Terry.”
</p>

<p>
“Terry what?”
</p>

<p>
“Just Terry.”
</p>

<p>
“You don’t trust me.” She said it quietly, but she
was hurt.
</p>

<p>
“Terry Fleming.” He spoke the name gently and
Laura saw a look on his face that changed him entirely.
She studied him, surprised.
</p>

<p>
“Jack, I think you’re in love.” Once said, it
sounded gauche and unfair.
</p>

<p>
But he only said, “I think so, too.”
</p>

<p>
Laura was lost. What do you do on the spot like this?
“I don’t know whether to give you my congratulations
or my sympathy,” she said seriously.
</p>

<p>
Jack laughed. “Both, Mother. That’s a beautiful sentiment,
whatever it is. Thanks.”
</p>

<p>
He seemed unable to talk about it and Laura finally
returned to Sarah. “Is there somebody in the office you
<a class="pagenum" title="121" id="page-121"></a>
could get for Sarah?” she asked. She described her to
him. “She’s not pretty, but she’s just a swell girl.”
</p>

<p>
“I know, there are a million of ’em,” he sighed. “I
wish to God I were straight. I’d marry her, poor kid.”
Laura stared at him, then smiled. “I guess I can arrange
something,” he said. “Do we have to double with
them?”
</p>

<p>
“She expects it. I hate to ask.”
</p>

<p>
“Okay, okay. It won’t kill me. But dinner only. And
I’ll be in a hurry.”
</p>

<p>
“Thanks, Jack.”
</p>

<p>
“I’d better find a tame one for her,” he mused and
then laughed a little. “Whatever that means. Jesus, the
poor girl has probably dreamed all her life of a good
thorough raping. But I can’t assume the responsibility.
Maybe Jensen can go. I’ll call you in the morning
on it.”
</p>

<p>
“Thanks. She’d be so happy.” Laura finished a second
drink. Jack was two up on her. She looked at him
out of the corner of her eye, wanting to tell him about
the change that had come over Marcie but afraid of his
sarcasm. Finally she said, in the characteristic blunt
way that disguised her uncertainty, “Jack, Marcie is
different. Something’s happening to her. I—I’m
scared.” She could go no further. She looked away from
him.
</p>

<p>
Jack chuckled. “Well, what’s she doing, filching undies
from Macy’s basement?”
</p>

<p>
“She doesn’t want to see Burr any more.”
</p>

<p>
Jack frowned slightly. “She’s finally coming to her
senses? That was a screwy match to begin with. Burr
wants to worship one gal. Marcie wants to raise a little
hell with every other man she sees.”
</p>

<p>
“She wants to be like me. That’s what she said. She
wants to read books. Spend more time at home. She
wants me to help her.”
</p>

<p>
“Help her what?”
</p>

<p>
Laura frowned. “I don’t know what. She says she’s sick
of herself and she wants to be a better person.”
</p>

<p>
Jack bit his underlip reflectively. “I know what
you’re thinking. And you’re wrong. She’s not turning
gay.”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="122" id="page-122"></a>
“I didn’t say that.” Laura turned to him indignantly.
</p>

<p>
“You don’t have to. You’re thinking it so hard I can
hear the wheels going around in your head.” He looked
at her. “Once and for all, Laura, she’s <em>not gay</em>. Maybe
she’s got room in her somewhere for a little curiosity.
Maybe living with you really has made her dissatisfied
with herself. If so, so much the better. But she’s
not mooning for you every night.”
</p>

<p>
“She acted so funny, Jack. Like—like she enjoyed having
me near her. Like she wanted me to touch her. I
mean, comfort her. You know.”
</p>

<p>
“The more you want her to enjoy it, the more it’ll
seem like she does.”
</p>

<p>
“I’m not making up stories,” Laura said with some
heat.
</p>

<p>
“No, Mother, I know. I believe you. I’m just telling
you a fact. I’ve known Marcie for a couple of years.
From the time she and Burr started dating right
through their divorce. She’s capable of—let’s say—wondering.
Like the night she wanted to touch tongues.”
Laura shivered involuntarily. “Once in a while she
gets a kick out of a fling in the Village. Maybe she
just wants to see how far you’ll go. Maybe she’s egging
you on, Laura. Did you ever think of that? Just to see
what the hell you’ll do?”
</p>

<p>
“She wouldn’t do that,” Laura said positively, somewhat
shocked. “Never.”
</p>

<p>
Jack gave a little snort. “Okay, maybe not. But she’s
not about to fall for you. Not now or ever.”
</p>

<p>
“She meant it when she talked to me last night. She
was sincere.”
</p>

<p>
“Sure she meant it. She’s on a book kick. She’s obviously
very impressed with you. It shows when she
talks about you. Temporarily, you’re somebody to imitate,
somebody to admire.”
</p>

<p>
“Temporarily?”
</p>

<p>
“Don’t fall into a trap.” He put a hand on her knee.
</p>

<p>
“It’s no trap! She’s too innocent to set me a trap.”
</p>

<p>
“Innocent?” He laughed. “Don’t count on it. Besides,
you’re too innocent to avoid one. Right now you have
a lot to learn.”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="123" id="page-123"></a>
Laura glared at him. “I’m not stupid.”
</p>

<p>
“No, you’re not. You’re very bright, honey. You’re just
uninformed. If you want to learn, go scout up Beebo and
take the Grand Tour of the Village.”
</p>

<p>
“I could no more fall for her than I could fall for—for—”
</p>

<p>
“For me?” He laughed.
</p>

<p>
She smiled suddenly and laughed with him. “Oh,”
she said with a wave of her hand, “You’re taken.”
</p>

<p>
“Mother, that’s a beautiful one-line definition of
my dilemma. My analyst could use you.”
</p>

<p>
“Are you being analyzed?” she asked.
</p>

<p>
“Aren’t we all?”
</p>

<p>
“Answer me!”
</p>

<p>
“I did. Let’s go. I hate to keep people waiting.”
</p>

<p>
“Who’s waiting?”
</p>

<p>
“My friend, Mr. Fleming.”
</p>

<p>
She slipped off the barstool, pulling her jacket on.
“I’d like to meet him.”
</p>

<p>
“If it lasts another couple of weeks, I guess it’d be
safe.” He took her arm and steered her through the crowd.
They stared at her but it didn’t disconcert her so much
now that she had had a drink or two.
</p>

<p>
“Where’s your adoring roommate tonight?” Jack said as
they went up the stairs.
</p>

<p>
“Having dinner downtown.”
</p>

<p>
“Why don’t you go over to The Cellar? Let Beebo tell
you some fairy stories. She’s got a million of ’em.”
</p>

<p>
“I couldn’t take Beebo tonight.”
</p>

<p>
“Suit yourself. I’ll walk you to the subway.”
</p>

<p>
“No, don’t bother. I know you want to get home.
Thanks a lot, Jack. I don’t know why you’re so good to
me.”
</p>

<p>
“My interest is purely academic. Your innocence
amazes me.”
</p>

<p>
“You make me feel like a hayseed,” she said.
</p>

<p>
He laughed. “Okay, Hayseed. I’ll call you in the morning
on what’s-her-name.”
</p>

<p>
“Sarah.”
</p>

<p>
“Sarah. See you.” And he turned and walked off.
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
Laura walked toward the subway but she knew she
wasn’t going home. She knew she would walk right past
<a class="pagenum" title="124" id="page-124"></a>
it and she did. She walked for four blocks, seething
with a renewed fury at Merrill Landon. Her hot hand
was cramped around the slip of paper with Beebo’s
number on it, in the pocket of her jacket....
</p>

<p>
<em>I’ll pay her back. I’ll just give her the money I owe
her, have one drink, and go home.</em> She looked at her
watch—a little past nine. For a moment she stood at
the head of the stairs looking down at the double doors
that opened into The Cellar, feeling her heart bound
nervously. She never seemed able to walk into this place
confidently. There was always a moment of fear and reluctance.
But the need to be with her own kind quickly
overpowered it.
</p>

<p>
She walked in, heading for the bar, ignoring the
curious stares that greeted her. She stood at the end of the
bar and when the bartender came up he recognized her.
</p>

<p>
“Hi,” he said. “What’ll it be?”
</p>

<p>
“Whisky and water, please,” she said. She looked
around the place, up and down the bar, around the
tables, but she didn’t see Beebo. She drank half her drink,
and then walked back to the ladies’ room, looking into
the rear of The Cellar, but Beebo wasn’t there. In extreme
irritation she walked back to the bar, wondering
whether to crush her pride and ask the bartender where
Beebo was, or let it go. She finished her drink and decided
if she had come this far she might as well go
the whole way.
</p>

<p>
“Where’s Beebo tonight?” she asked the bartender the
next time he got near. “I owe her some money,” she explained
compulsively. He smiled.
</p>

<p>
“Oh, she’s been and gone already,” he said. “She’s
probably over at The Colophon. She likes it over there.
No boys.” He grinned.
</p>

<p>
“Thanks,” said Laura, slipping off her seat at the
bar and heading for the door. She was embarrassed
enough without asking him where The Colophon was.
She didn’t want to advertise her “innocence.”
</p>

<p>
Near the door a slim pretty girl, who had kept an eye
on her at the bar, approached her. The girl wore her
hair in a short soft curly cut. She was blonde and feminine.
Laura let herself be approached simply by returning
the girl’s gaze as she came near her. She stopped
when the girl spoke.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="125" id="page-125"></a>
“Excuse me,” the girl said. “We noticed you were
all alone. My friends and me. Like to have a drink
with us?” She nodded toward a table where three other
girls were sitting, watching them. One of them, sitting
alone on one side of the table, stared coldly at
Laura.
</p>

<p>
Laura was flattered. But the feelings in her were too
personal, too rough, to dissipate with strangers.
“Thanks,” she said. “I’d like to, but I’m looking for
somebody. I’m in a hurry.”
</p>

<p>
“Who’re you looking for? Maybe we can help you
out,” said the girl, stopping Laura as she started to
move away.
</p>

<p>
Laura realized the girl was interested in her, and
it made her turn back once more. “Oh,” she said with
a little shrug, “you wouldn’t know her.”
</p>

<p>
“Somebody might. We’re over here a lot. What’s her
name?”
</p>

<p>
Laura was dead certain they’d know Beebo, who came
over here all the time. The last thing she wanted was
to have everybody run up and tell Beebo that Laura had
been looking for her. The bartender would no doubt tell
her. That was bad enough.
</p>

<p>
“What’s her name?” the girl prompted, and then
smiled. “Don’t want to tell?”
</p>

<p>
Laura blushed and backed away from her. “I just owe
her some money. I thought she’d be down here tonight.”
</p>

<p>
“Who?” the girl goaded her, with a pretty smile.
</p>

<p>
“Beebo Brinker.” Laura didn’t mean to say it. Yet
saying it was better than trying to hide it and getting
laughed at. They could always ask the bartender
who she was looking for after she left, and she would
look even worse. They would take her stammering reluctance
for infatuation. She said the name as casually
as she could.
</p>

<p>
“Oh, Beebo!” The girl laughed. “She left half an hour
ago. She’s over at the Colophon. She said this place
was dead tonight. I guess if she’d known you were coming
she would have waited—hm?” She smiled.
</p>

<p>
“I guess,” said Laura briefly. She stared at the girl.
It occurred to her that she saw a slight resemblance to
Beth in her face. Then she turned and walked out.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="126" id="page-126"></a>
The slim girl walked back to her table. Her partner
said peevishly, “Maybe that’ll teach you you’re not irresistible.”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, shut up,” the slim girl said quietly. “She’s
Beebo Brinker’s girl.”
</p>

<p>
“Beebo’s girl, hell. Beebo’s got a dozen girls. She can
really pick ’em though, I’ll say that much. I should get
one like that.” And she made a face at the slim one by
her side.
</p>

<div class="chapter">

<h2 class="chapter" id="chapter-0-11">
<a class="pagenum" title="127" id="page-127"></a>
Chapter Eleven
</h2>

</div>

<p class="first">
<span class="firstchar">L</span><span class="postfirstchar">aura</span> went home. She arrived before ten, but Marcie
wasn’t back yet. Laura put a book she had been reading
on Marcie’s bed and climbed into her own bed. She
tried to read herself, but she couldn’t. An hour went
by, and no Marcie. Nervously, Laura shut her book and
dropped it to the floor. She got up and went into the
bathroom to brush her teeth, and remembered she had
already done it.
</p>

<p>
Then she went to the phone. She didn’t know what
was coming over her. She only felt a deep will-defying
unhappiness. She pulled out the phone book and looked
up the number of the McAlton Hotel. She sat for a moment
with the book open in her lap, unable to move. Then
she reached slowly for the phone.
</p>

<p>
Suddenly it rang. Laura screamed, a small quick cry
of extreme surprise. Her heart had taken a tremendous
leap at the piercing bell sound in that still apartment.
She let it ring twice more while she caught her breath.
<em>It must be Marcie</em>, she thought. <em>Maybe she’s in trouble.</em>
She lifted the receiver. “Hello?”
</p>

<p>
“Hello, Bo-peep.”
</p>

<p>
Laura’s heart gave another bound. She felt the sweat
break out. “Beebo?” she said faintly.
</p>

<p>
“How are you, sweetheart? I hear you were looking for
me tonight.”
</p>

<p>
“You didn’t waste any time.” Her voice was sharp.
</p>

<p>
“I hate to keep a lady waiting. What’s on your
mind?”
</p>

<p>
“I just dropped in for a drink. I was down there in
the Village to see Jack and I just wanted to pay you
back.” She spoke in fits and starts.
</p>

<p>
“You don’t owe me a thing, Bo-peep. Not a thing.”
</p>

<p>
“A drink.” Laura hated to owe anybody anything.
She was meticulous about her debts, however small the
sum.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="128" id="page-128"></a>
“You’re right.” Laura could feel her smile. “I nearly
forgot. Okay lover, you owe me one drink.”
</p>

<p>
“Beebo, I can’t talk now, really.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re doing fine. What’s the matter, Marcie
breathing down your neck?”
</p>

<p>
“It’s not that.”
</p>

<p>
“You don’t have to say you love me, you know. Just
say you’ll meet me tomorrow night. About eight.”
</p>

<p>
“No.”
</p>

<p>
“Don’t be late, doll. I’ll call Marcie and ask her where
the hell you are.”
</p>

<p>
“You wouldn’t! You won’t! Damn you, Beebo!”
</p>

<p>
“I would and I will.” She laughed. “Eight on the
dot.”
</p>

<p>
“I won’t be there.”
</p>

<p>
“Want to bet?”
</p>

<p>
Laura hung up on her. She was trembling. Angrily
she slammed the heavy phone back into place, switched
out the bedroom light, and got into bed.
</p>

<p>
The black night settled around her but it brought
more restless tossing than repose. The hours slipped by.
No Marcie. No sleep. Only an endless bitter reviewing
of what her father had done to her; the look on the clerk’s
face when he gave her the message; the impotent fury
and shame that besieged her. At last she turned the light
back on and began to pace the room. The electric clock
on Marcie’s dresser said two-thirty. Laura wondered
whether to call Burr. Or Jack. She was getting afraid
for Marcie. But nobody knew how to reach her. There
was nothing to do but wait.
</p>

<p>
It was a few minutes past three when Marcie came
in. Laura had left the living room light on for her
and she heard her come in laughing and heard a male
voice answer her. Not Burr’s voice. Somebody else. A
deep mature voice. Laura peeked out through the crack
in the kitchen door but couldn’t see him. Marcie was
giggling, as if she were tight, and pushing him away.
Laura could see her now and then.
</p>

<p>
Marcie said, “I’ll call my roommate. She’ll make you
go home.”
</p>

<p>
“I can’t go home tonight. I live in Chicago.”
</p>

<p>
“That’s where she’s from!”
</p>

<p>
“Who?”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="129" id="page-129"></a>
“My roommate.”
</p>

<p>
“To hell with her. Come here, Baby.”
</p>

<p>
“No!” High as she was, she nevertheless sounded a
little scared. She had stopped laughing.
</p>

<p>
Laura threw a coat hastily over her pajamas and went
into the living room. A large man, partly bald and handsome
in a heavy featured way, had Marcie wrapped in
a bear hug and was trying to drag her to the couch.
</p>

<p>
“All right,” said Laura sharply. “Get out.”
</p>

<p>
She startled them both so much that they froze where
they were. The man stared at her. He was drunk, and his
balance wasn’t the best. Laura, pale and silver blond, her
long hair falling down her shoulders, her face strange
and sensitive and imperious, looked like an apparition
to him. Without taking his eyes off her he
asked Marcie, “Who the hell is <em>that</em>?”
</p>

<p>
“My roommate.” Marcie took advantage of his interest
to slip free. Laura took her arm firmly and sent
her through the kitchen door. Then she turned back to
the man.
</p>

<p>
“All right, you,” she said as if he were a servant.
“Out.”
</p>

<p>
The impudence of it amused him and angered him
at the same time. “You can’t talk to me like that,” he
said.
</p>

<p>
She advanced on him briskly, pulling the door open
sharply and facing him. “It’s my home and I’ll speak
as I please,” she said. She looked as cold and unapproachable
as she was hot and angry. He stared at her,
not sure how to take her, and then came toward her to
put a hand on her shoulder.
</p>

<p>
“Marcie’s no good,” he said confidentially. “Let’s
you and me—”
</p>

<p>
Laura swept his hand off her shoulder. “Get out of
here or I’ll call the police,” she said.
</p>

<p>
He got mad. “Jesus, what a chilly little bitch you
are!” he growled.
</p>

<p>
“Get out,” Laura said, so cold, so controlled, that she
froze him into submission. She shut the door after
him, resisting the urge to slam it. <em>Dear God</em>, she
thought intensely. <em>If I could do that to my father. Just
once.</em>
</p>

<p>
“Laura? It was just a party, Laur,” Marcie said. “We
<a class="pagenum" title="130" id="page-130"></a>
went out after dinner. Just for kicks. He got sort of out
of hand. Thanks, Laur, I don’t know what I’d have done.”
</p>

<p>
“Come on to bed.” Laura turned and walked toward
her and Marcie preceded her into the bedroom. It occurred
to her then that she was behaving with Marcie
much as Beth used to behave with her. She was asserting
herself, taking the lead. She liked it; with Marcie,
anyway. She felt her influence and reveled in it. A
feeling of tremendous strength swept through her when
the man turned and left, like the other poor demented
little fellow who pestered her on the subway. Only he
was such a weakling he hardly counted. She had a mental
image of herself treating Merrill Landon that way, and
it worked a strange exaltation in her. She smiled.
</p>

<p>
Marcie grinned at her crookedly. “I thought you’d
be sore,” she said.
</p>

<p>
“No. No, of course not. Why should I be?”
</p>

<p>
“I don’t know. Maybe because I felt kind of guilty
going out with somebody besides Burr. But I had fun.
Up to the end, anyway. I wouldn’t have minded that if
he hadn’t slobbered so much.” She giggled and Laura
ignored what she said. They were standing less than a
foot from each other and suddenly Laura reached for her
and gave her a little hug. “I’m not mad. I’m just glad
you’re all right,” she said.
</p>

<p>
Marcie submitted, but she seemed embarrassed, and
Laura quickly released her. With the release came a
letdown, a loss of strength and confidence. She slipped
quietly into bed and spent the hours till dawn wrestling
with the bedclothes.
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
Laura didn’t feel much brighter than Marcie in the
morning. She got to work on time, but all she wanted to
do was sleep. <em>I’ve got to catch up. I’ve got to catch up</em>,
she kept telling herself. <em>Less than three weeks and Jean’ll
be back. And I haven’t done a really good day’s work
since she left. Even if they like me, they can’t keep me
on as a charity case.</em>
</p>

<p>
The episode at the McAlton flamed up in her mind
and gave her an angry energy through most of the morning.
Sarah said nothing to her, but she kept looking
at her over her typewriter, apparently afraid to bring
<a class="pagenum" title="131" id="page-131"></a>
up the date subject again. It wasn’t till Jack called that
Laura even remembered it.
</p>

<p>
“All set,” he said. “Carl Jensen can go. Friday night.
Dinner and a show. What’s Sarah’s number?”
</p>

<p>
Laura got it from her and made her face light up
with expectation. Jack put Carl on the phone, and
Laura gave her end to Sarah. It gave her a momentary
lift to see somebody else stammering with pleasure
and anticipation. But the day she lived through was
endless, bleak with undone work, dragging will, impotent
anger.
</p>

<p class="tb">
* * *
</p>

<p class="noindent">
“You’re late,” Marcie said when Laura walked in. “I
wanted you to tell me about that book Burr brought over
last week.”
</p>

<p>
“Nothing to tell.” Laura felt too low to talk, to joke,
even to eat. She picked listlessly at her food. After a while
Marcie fell silent, too.
</p>

<p>
When the dishes were done Marcie said, “I called Burr.
Broke our date tonight.” She looked expectantly at Laura,
as if this were a significant revelation, and she wanted a
proper reaction.
</p>

<p>
But Laura only said, “Oh?” and walked into the bedroom.
</p>

<p>
Marcie followed her. “What’s the matter, Laur?” she
said. And when Laura didn’t answer, she asked, “Bad
day?”
</p>

<p>
“Um-hm. Bad day.” Laura lay down on her bed, face
downward, one leg hanging over the edge, her mind
wholly occupied with her father: her hatred, her stifled
love for him, her fear of him.
</p>

<p>
“Talk to me, Laura,” Marcie said, coming over to sit
next to her.
</p>

<p>
“Not tonight.”
</p>

<p>
“Please. You said you would.”
</p>

<p>
“I can’t, Marcie. I can’t talk. I’m too tired.” She rolled
over and looked at her. “Don’t look like that,” she said.
“I’m—I’m worried about my job, that’s all. I’ll be all
right.”
</p>

<p>
“What’s wrong with your job?”
</p>

<p>
“Nothing.”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, Laura! God! Make sense!” Marcie exclaimed.
<a class="pagenum" title="132" id="page-132"></a>
But when she evoked no response she dropped it with a
sigh. “Let’s go out on the roof,” she said, “and get some
fresh air. It’s a beautiful night.”
</p>

<p>
“Looks like rain.”
</p>

<p>
“How would you know? You’re staring at the ceiling.”
</p>

<p>
“It did, earlier.”
</p>

<p>
“That’s what’s beautiful about it. Maybe there’ll be
thunder. I love to stand naked in the rain.” She
glanced down slowly at Laura.
</p>

<p>
But Laura turned back on her stomach without a word.
A terrible apathy nailed her to the bed. Not even the
nearness of Marcie could arouse her. They sat quietly for
a few minutes, Laura lost in herself, and Marcie searching
for a way to cheer her up. The phone rang.
</p>

<p>
“I’ll get it,” Marcie said, and got up. She walked across
the room and picked up the receiver when Laura suddenly
remembered Beebo. She sat up in a rush.
</p>

<p>
“No,” Marcie was saying, “I can’t. I’m sorry. I don’t
want to argue, not any more. I’ve had enough, that’s all.
I won’t talk to you, Burr. No, it’s not her fault, it’s nobody’s
fault.” She looked at Laura stretched out again
on her bed. “That has nothing to do with it. No. Goodnight,
Burr.”
</p>

<p>
She hung up and stood for a moment motionless, watching
Laura, who lay with her face turned away, apparently
relaxed. Burr was getting jealous, impatient. He was
ready to accuse anybody of anything to get Marcie’s favor
back. Their phone conversations were little more than arguments
which Marcie terminated by hanging up on him.
But he wouldn’t be put off for long.
</p>

<p>
Marcie sat down on her own bed with a book, the one
she meant to ask Laura about. She stared at the pages
without reading, and wondered about her moody roommate.
</p>

<p>
Laura was watching her wristwatch. It was two minutes
fast. She lay still, but she was alert, poised to jump. At
two minutes past eight, by her watch, the phone rang
again. “It’s for me,” she told Marcie, who had no intention
of going for it. Laura came across the room and sat
on Marcie’s bed.
</p>

<p>
“Hello?” she said into the receiver.
</p>

<p>
“Hi, lover. Where are you?”
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="133" id="page-133"></a>
“At home,” Laura said sarcastically. “Where else?”
</p>

<p>
“You want me to come over?”
</p>

<p>
“I’ll be down in a few minutes. I was delayed.”
</p>

<p>
“Okay, but make it fast. I’ll call again at eight-thirty.
And every ten minutes after that.”
</p>

<p>
Laura hung up without a further word and turned to
look at Marcie. “I met him at work,” she said, her face
flushing. “He’s been pestering me. I don’t want to see
him.” She didn’t know what she was going to do.
</p>

<p>
“Oh,” said Marcie. <em>Then why all the fuss?</em> She looked
curiously at Laura’s pink face. Laura turned away and
began to walk up and down the room, feeling as if there
were a bomb sealed in her breast, ticking, about to go off.
She knew her nails were cutting her underarms, yet she
hardly felt them. It was an expression of terrible tension
in her. Suddenly she whipped the closet door open and
pulled out her coat.
</p>

<p>
Marcie, watching her, said quickly, “Where’re you
going?”
</p>

<p>
“I’ll be back early,” Laura said, heading for the door,
propelled by the tight violence that was boiling inside her.
</p>

<p>
“Laura!” Marcie jumped up and followed her. “Damn
it, Laur, please tell me, I’m worried about you.”
</p>

<p>
Laura turned abruptly at the door. “I’m just going out
for a little while,” she said. “I won’t be late.” She tried to
leave, but Marcie grabbed her arms.
</p>

<p>
“You’re not fit to go anywhere, Laura. I never saw
you so upset,” Marcie said. “Except once. And you—you
spent the night with Jack that time. It was my fault. Is
this my fault? Am I driving you out again?”
</p>

<p>
“No, no, nothing’s your fault.” Laura covered her face
with her hand for a minute and when Marcie’s arms went
around her to comfort her, she wept. “Please don’t let me
go,” she whispered. “I mean—God!—I mean, let me go.
Let me go, Marcie.” She began to resist.
</p>

<p>
But the curiosity in Marcie had taken over. “You’re
trembling all over. Come to bed, Laur. Come on, honey,
you’re in no shape to go anywhere. Come tell me about
it,” she coaxed, trying to guide Laura away from the door.
But Laura knew what was in store for her if she obeyed.
She uncovered her face to gaze for a moment at Marcie,
so close to her, so tantalizing. And that terrible storm
<a class="pagenum" title="134" id="page-134"></a>
brewing inside her made her feel as if she might do any
wild thing that her body demanded of her. She was afraid.
</p>

<p>
“Please,” Marcie said softly. “I’ll give you a rubdown,
I’m a great masseuse. My father taught me how.” She
smiled. “Please, Laur.”
</p>

<p>
“Your father?”
</p>

<p>
“Yes.”
</p>

<p>
“Do you love him very much?”
</p>

<p>
“Yes.” Marcie frowned at her.
</p>

<p>
“And he loves you?”
</p>

<p>
“Of course.”
</p>

<p>
“You’re lucky, Marcie.”
</p>

<p>
“That’s the way it’s supposed to be, Laur. I’m not
lucky. I’m just normal. Ordinary, I mean.”
</p>

<p>
Laura stared at her. The emotion in her simmered dangerously
near the top. With a sudden swift movement,
Laura kissed Marcie’s cheek lightly, leaving the wet of her
tears on Marcie’s face, and then whispered, “So lucky ...
so lucky ...” Then she turned and ran down the stairs
to the elevator.
</p>

<p>
Marcie sat down on a living room chair and put her
head in her hands and tried to think. Laura’s strange behaviour
made her tickle inside. She felt close to the storm
that had barely brushed past her, and yet she remained
untouched. There was only the wet on her cheek as a

Laura smiled, surprised.
</p>

<p>
“Well?” said Beebo, when she hung up. “Going
home to your little wife?” She grinned.
</p>

<p>
“Beebo, sometimes you make me sick.”
</p>

<p>
“I know. I’m enough to make you go straight. Go sleep
with Jack tonight, it’ll do him a world of good.”
</p>

<p>
“Oh, shut up!”
</p>

<p>
“At least it’ll give him a whopper to tell his analyst.”
</p>

<p>
Laura turned on her heel and left the room. She felt
her clothes, hanging in the bathroom. They were still
damp, but dry enough to iron. She brought them into
the kitchen. “Where’s the ironing board?” she said.
</p>

<p>
“Pretty determined, aren’t you?”
</p>

<p>
“I certainly am.”
</p>

<p>
“I’ve got dinner ready. You can eat before you go.”
There was a faint tone of pleading in her voice, as
if she knew the time had gone when sheer force was
useless. Laura had made her mind up.
</p>

<p>
“I don’t want another thing from you, not even dinner.”
</p>

<p>
“No, not for another day or two,” Beebo said and her
voice became rougher as she talked. “You just want to
run down for kicks once or twice a week. I’m pretty
damn convenient, aren’t I?” She pulled the board out
from the wall and plugged the iron in, her movements
sharp and angry. Laura felt a little afraid of her. Her
blue eyes snapped and there was no trace of her usual
humor in her face.
</p>

<p>
“You’re the bitch, Laura, not me. You’re using me,”
she said. “Go on, iron the damn thing.” She waved a
hand at Laura’s dress and Laura spread it out on the
board.
</p>

<p>
“I’m sorry, Beebo,” Laura said, taken aback.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="146" id="page-146"></a>
“Sure you are.”
</p>

<p>
“All right, Beebo,” she said softly. “I won’t bother
you any more. Ever.”
</p>

<p>
Beebo snorted at her. “You try it and I’ll beat you, I
swear I will,” she said. “I’ve had enough from you,
Laura. I’m not made of stone. Am I nothing to you?
Am I supposed to <em>believe</em> I’m nothing to you? Do you
think I like to stand and listen to you slobber over
that simpering little roommate of yours? Can she give
you what I can give you? Well damn it, <em>can</em> she?”
</p>

<p>
Laura couldn’t face her, much less answer her. She only
worked the iron over her dress and glanced at Beebo’s
shoes.
</p>

<p>
Beebo’s voice softened a little. “Jesus, what a mess,”
she said, leaning on the refrigerator. “Here I am falling
for you. I ought to have my head examined. I ought to
know better.” She came over to Laura and took the iron
out of her hands and Laura had to look at her. “Laura,”
Beebo said, leaning toward her, “I’m nuts for you. I
wasn’t kidding.” They gazed at each other, Laura surprised
and scared and flattered all at once. “I need you,
baby,” Beebo whispered. “Please stay.”
</p>

<p>
“I can’t, Beebo,” Laura said.
</p>

<p>
“You don’t really think you’re in love with that little
blonde, do you?”
</p>

<p>
“Yes.”
</p>

<p>
Beebo shook her head and shut her eyes for a minute.
“Jack says she’s straight. Jack is a shrewd boy. Don’t
you believe him?”
</p>

<p>
“No.”
</p>

<p>
“You want to get the Miseries, Baby? That’s the
quickest way.”
</p>

<p>
“You don’t know her, Beebo. Even Jack doesn’t know
her as I do. She’s changing. She seems interested in
me. She’s sort of approachable. She doesn’t even want to
see her ex-husband anymore. She wants to stay home at
night with me. She breaks dates with him to do it.”
</p>

<p>
“All right.” Beebo turned away. “Suppose she’s gay.
Suppose she is. What then?” She turned to look sharply
at Laura.
</p>

<p>
Laura was stumped. She had never looked beyond the
present into that possibility. What would it be like,
<a class="pagenum" title="147" id="page-147"></a>
just the two of them, both gay, living together, in love?
“Well, then everything will be wonderful,” she said.
</p>

<p>
Beebo gave a short unpleasant laugh. “Yeah,” she said.
“Wonderful. You walk hand in hand into the sunset.”
</p>

<p>
“I didn’t mean to hurt you, Beebo. I never made a secret
of my feelings for Marcie.”
</p>

<p>
“I never made a secret of mine for you, baby.”
</p>

<p>
“We’d never do anything but fight, Beebo.”
</p>

<p>
“Fight and make love. I could live forever on such
a diet.” She smiled a little.
</p>

<p>
“It would drive me crazy. I couldn’t take it.”
</p>

<p>
“Do you think there won’t be fights with your little
Marcie if she turns out gay?”
</p>

<p>
“I suppose there will.”
</p>

<p>
“You know damn well there will. And if she’s
straight, what happens? She reads you the Riot Act.
Calls the cops. Sics her husband on you.”
</p>

<p>
“She wouldn’t do any of those things, Beebo. She’s a
sweet girl. She wouldn’t get wild like you.”
</p>

<p>
“Not according to Jack. You’ve known her four
months. Jack’s known her for years.” Beebo lighted a
cigarette and blew the smoke through her nose. “Want
to know something, Bo-peep? Want to know what it’s
like? I’ve had it happen to me—more than once. If
you’re gay, it just happens now and then, that’s all. You
get the bug for some lovely kid and you can’t keep it
to yourself. You get closer and closer. And if she plays
along it’s worse and worse. And finally you give in
and you grab for her. And she turns to ice in your arms.”
</p>

<p>
She looked at Laura and there was a deep regret in her
eyes. “And she gets up with the God-damnedest sort of
dignity and walks across the room and says ‘I’m sorry.
I’m so sorry for you. Now go away. Don’t talk, don’t try
to explain, I don’t want to hear. It makes me sick. Just
go away, and I won’t tell our friends. You don’t need to
worry. Just so I never see you again.’ It makes you heartsick,
baby. You get so sick inside. You give yourself
the heaves. All you want in God’s world is to get the
hell out of your own skin and be normal. Fade into the
crowd like a normal nobody.” She crushed her cigarette
out, grinding it into the ashtray with her thumb till
the paper burst and the brown tobacco spilled out.
</p>

<p>
<a class="pagenum" title="148" id="page-148"></a>
Laura felt closer to her. All the insults of the day faded
in her mind. She walked over to her, her pressed dress
over one shoulder. “Beebo,” she said softly.
</p>

<p>
But Beebo wasn’t ready to let herself be touched. “Just
remember one thing,” she said. “Too many Marcies
in your life, and you commit suicide. That’s what it
is to be gay, Laura. Gay.” Laura stepped back a little
shocked. “Sometimes all it takes is one,” Beebo said.
</p>

<p>
“No,” Laura whispered. “Oh, no.”
</p>

<p>
“Okay, baby, go find out for yourself. I can’t stop you,
Jack can’t stop you.” Beebo’s eyes were brilliant with
bitterness, with the hard knowledge of her own experience.
“Go play with your little blonde. You’ll find
out soon enough she has claws. And teeth. And when you
get to playing the wrong games with her, she’ll use them.”
</p>

<p>
“Never!” Laura said. “Even if she’s straight she
won’t hurt me. She’s not that kind.”
</p>

<p>
“She doesn’t <em>have</em> to hurt you, idiot. Can’t I get that
through your head? All she has to do is say ‘no thanks’.
Kindly. Sympathetically. Hell! If you want her bad
enough, you’ll die of it. I know, Laura, I know!” And
she took Laura’s shoulders and shook her head until
Laura felt like sobbing. Beebo released her suddenly
and they stood in silence, unable to talk, heavy with
feeling, trembling.
</p>

<p>
Finally Beebo said quietly, “Go on, baby. Go home
and get it over with. You’ve been warned.” All the fight
seemed gone out of her.
</p>

<p>
When Laura left, Beebo came to the door with Nix
at her heels. She was unsmiling. “Come back, baby,”
she said. “To stay. Or don’t come back at all.” And when
Laura turned away without answering she called after
her, “I mean it!”
</p>

<div class="chapter">

<h2 class="chapter" id="chapter-0-12">
<a class="pagenum" title="149" id="page-149"></a>
Chapter Twelve
</h2>

</div>

<p class="first">
<span class="firstchar">L</span><span class="postfirstchar">aura</span> entered the penthouse and walked slowly back
to the bedroom. It was hard to imagine Marcie’s mood.
Marcie looked up from her bed, her hair in pincurls.
She was a relief to Laura’s eyes after the stormy, ranting
handsomeness of Beebo. Marcie looked beautiful, even
with tin clips in her hair. But she looked cool, too;
ready for a fight.
</p>

<p>
Laura slipped her jacket off without a word, thinking
of the loud quarrels she and Beth used to have. And
how they resolved them with love. A little curl of excitement
twisted around her innards.
</p>

<p>
“Well?” Marcie said sharply. “Did he throw you down
in the street?”
</p>

<p>
Laura was startled, offended. Marcie had no right to
say such a thing. “What do you mean?” she said.
</p>

<p>
“Your dress,” Marcie said, nodding at it.
</p>

<p>
Laura looked down at it. Beebo had dragged it over the
